The Invalidity of Natural Properties as Causes
Baqillani concludes that natural properties cannot cause events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that natural properties cannot cause events, based on the principle that accidents cannot be agents.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot act; natural properties are accidents, so they cannot cause anything.

This lesson presents Baqillani's concluding argument against the naturalist view that properties in things have inherent causal power. Building on earlier proofs that accidents cannot be agents of actions, he shows that the claim of natural properties causing events is invalid. Let's examine how he connects these ideas.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Baqillani first describes the naturalists' claim and then invokes a principle.
The passage is a conclusion, so it references earlier arguments.
2
Identify the two key parts
First, Baqillani says the naturalists' claim is not like true causation or generation. Second, he states the established principle that accidents cannot be agents.
The word 'ثابت' indicates the principle is already proven.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since natural properties are accidents, they cannot act. Therefore, their claim to cause events is invalid (بطل).
This is a direct application of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التي في هذه الأشياء بزعمهم موجبةً لهذه الأمور، لا على حد إيجاب العلة للحكم، ولا على سبيل ما تولد عن سبب يوجبه، على مذهب أصحاب التولد . وقد ثبت أيضاً بما قدمناه أنه لا يجوز أن تكون الأعراض فاعلة للأفعال ؛ فبطل ما يثبتونه من فعل الطبائع أو إيجابها لهذه الحوادث .
...which they claim are necessitating these things, not in the manner of a cause necessitating its effect, nor in the manner of that which is generated from a cause that necessitates it, according to the school of those who believe in generation. And it has also been established by what we have presented that it is not permissible for accidents to be agents of actions; so what they affirm of the action of natural properties or their necessitation of these events is invalidated.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion drawn from previous arguments. It begins by describing the claim of the naturalists: they assert that properties in things necessitate their effects, but not in the manner of a true cause (علة) necessitating its effect, nor in the manner of generated effects (تولد) as held by the Mu'tazila. Then the author invokes a previously established principle: accidents (أعراض) cannot be agents of actions. Since natural properties are accidents (according to Ash'ari ontology), they cannot act. Therefore, the claim that natural properties cause events is invalid.

Key Terms
طبائع
أعراض
تولد
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris deny all causality entirely.
Correction: Baqillani focuses on the impossibility of accidents acting as agents; he is not denying that Allah creates events in a customary sequence.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the underlying principle that invalidates the claim of natural properties causing events?
A) Accidents cannot be agents of actions.
B) Fire does not burn by nature.
C) The Mu'tazila are wrong about generation.
D) Knowledge of causation is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Natural properties cannot cause events because they are accidents, and accidents cannot be agents of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your own skills, health, or circumstances as independent causes of success or failure, forgetting that they are accidents that cannot act? How would your daily reliance on Allah change if you truly internalized that nothing in creation can cause anything without His will?
?
When you face a setback, do you blame a 'natural cause' (e.g., bad luck, someone's behavior, a system) as if it had power over you? How does Baqillani's refutation of natural properties as agents challenge you to turn immediately to Allah in that moment?
?
You have just learned that accidents cannot act. Yet you still act as if your own decisions and efforts are the real causes of your outcomes. What would it cost you to live today with the conviction that only Allah brings about every effect—and to act accordingly?

Refuting Necessity of Causal Knowledge in Sensation
Baqillani refutes claim that burning and intoxication are known by necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the cause of burning and intoxication cannot be known through sense experience alone.

Core Idea
Sense perception shows change, not its cause.

This passage tackles a fundamental misconception about causality. Many assume that when we see fire burn, we directly know that the fire causes the burning. Baqillani dismantles this assumption by clarifying what we actually observe versus what requires deeper investigation. He then presents four competing explanations for the observed change, showing that the cause is not immediately apparent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the strong opening dismissal and the shift to analysis.
The Arabic uses 'يهذون' (babbling) to set a confrontational tone.
2
Distinguish observation from inference
Identify Baqillani's key move: he separates what is directly sensed (the change) from what is known by investigation (the cause). This is the core of his refutation.
3
List the four competing views
Find and list the four different opinions about the cause of the change. Notice how each view attributes causality to a different agent or principle.
The views are: (1) from God, (2) from human act via generation, (3) from a natural property in the body, (4) from the property as an accident.
4
Understand the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that since there is such disagreement about the true cause, it cannot be known by simple observation. Reflect on why this refutes the claim of necessity.
This passage is a foundational argument for Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما يهذون به كثيراً من أنهم يعلمون حِسّاً واضطراراً أن الإحراق والإسكار الحادثين واقعان عن حرارة النار وشدة الشراب فإنه جهل عظيم وذلك أن الذي نشاهده ونحسه إنما هو تغير حال الجسم عند تناول الشراب ومجاورة النار وكونه سكران ومحترقاً ومتغيراً عما كان عليه فقط ; فأما العلم بأن هذه الحالة الحادثة المتجددة من فِعْلِ مَنْ هِيَ، فإنه غير مشاهَدٍ بل مُدْرَك بدقيق الفحص والبحث فمن قائل: إنه من قديم مخترع قادر وهو الحق الذي نذهب إليه ; ومن قائل يقول : إنه من فعل الإنسان الذي جاور النار وتناول الشرابَ ومتولّد عن فعله الذي هو سبب الإحراق والإسكار; ومن قائل يقول إنه فعل الطبع في الجسم ولا أدري أهو نفس الجسم المطبوع أم معنى فيه ; ومن قائل يقول : إن الطبع عرض من الأعراض. فكيف يُدْرَك حقيقةُ ما قد اختلف فيه هذا الضرب من الاختلاف بالمشاهدة ودَرْكِ الحواس؟
As for what they frequently babble about, that they know by sense and necessity that the burning and intoxication that occur are brought about by the heat of fire and the strength of wine, this is great ignorance. For what we witness and sense is only the change of the body's state upon consuming the drink and proximity to fire, and its being drunk and burnt and changed from what it was, nothing more. As for knowledge of this new occurring state, from whose act it is, it is not observed but rather known by careful examination and investigation. Some say it is from an eternal, invented, powerful being – that is the truth which we hold. Some say it is from the act of the human who was near the fire and consumed the drink, generated from his act which is the cause of burning and intoxication. Some say it is the action of the natural property in the body, and I do not know whether it is the body itself having the property or a meaning in it. Some say that the natural property is an accident among accidents. So how can the reality of what is disputed with this kind of disagreement be known by observation and sense perception?
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the empiricist argument. The author first dismisses the claim as 'great ignorance'. He explains that sensory observation only reveals the change in the body, not the cause. To know the cause requires investigation, and there is great disagreement: the correct view (Ash'ari) attributes it to God; some attribute it to human action via generation; some to a natural property in the body; others say the property is an accident. Given this disagreement, how can one claim to know the cause by simple observation? Thus, the claim of necessity is false.

Key Terms
علم ضروري
مشاهدة
حواس
إحراق
إسكار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We directly perceive that fire causes burning and wine causes intoxication.
Correction: We only perceive the change in the body; the cause is not directly sensed but requires rational investigation.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani claim is the only thing observed in the case of fire and wine?
A) The heat of fire and strength of wine.
B) The change in the body's state.
C) The act of God.
D) The natural property.

Key Takeaway
The cause of observed events is not directly sensed; it requires rational investigation, as shown by the multiplicity of opinions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a good outcome—like passing an exam or recovering from illness—do you immediately attribute it to your own effort or to natural causes, without acknowledging that the true cause is Allah? How would your gratitude change if you truly believed that every effect is directly from Him?
?
This passage shows that even intelligent people disagree on the cause of simple events like burning. Are you quick to assume you know the causes of events in your life? Will you commit to pausing and remembering that the real cause may be beyond your observation, and that careful reflection is needed?
?
The author calls the opposing view 'great ignorance.' Is there an area in your life where you claim certainty based on what you've seen, while ignoring the deeper investigation required? What would it cost you to humbly admit that you don't know the ultimate cause, and to seek it from the One who does?

Baqillani's Refutation of Necessary Natural Causation
Baqillani argues that if natural causation were necessary, its denial would also be necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a symmetry argument and an analogy to refute the claim that knowledge of natural causation is necessary.

Core Idea
If natural causation were necessary, its denial would also be necessary — an absurd contradiction.

Baqillani here tackles a central claim of naturalists: that we know natural causation by necessity. He responds with a clever symmetry argument — if the naturalist can claim necessary knowledge, we can claim necessary knowledge of its falsity. Then he strengthens his case by comparing natural causation to truly undeniable truths, showing that the widespread denial of natural causation proves it is not necessary at all.

Guided Path
1
Read Baqillani's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Baqillani structures his refutation in two parts: a direct symmetry argument and an analogy with undeniable necessary truths.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا حيلة لهم في الخلاص منه' — he claims no escape.
2
Analyze the symmetry argument
Identify the conditional: if the naturalist can claim necessary knowledge of causation, then we can claim necessary knowledge that he is false. This creates a contradiction, refuting the original claim.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the analogy with necessary truths
Baqillani gives examples of things truly known by necessity: the sky above us, the earth below us, hearing the speaker. These cannot be denied by any rational community. But natural causation is widely denied, so it cannot be a necessary truth.
4
Draw the conclusion
The widespread denial of natural causation throughout history proves that it is not necessary knowledge. The claimant's ignorance is exposed by the fact that most people reject his claim.
Baqillani uses empirical observation of disagreement as evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز لزاعم أن يزعم أنه يعلم صحة فعل الطبع في الجسم لما حدث من هذه الأمور اضطراراً لجاز لنا أن ندعي أنا نعلم كذب مُدَّعي ذلك اضطراراً وأنه هو مضطرّ إلى ذلك ; وهذا مما لا حيلة لهم في الخلاص منه، وقد ثبت أن ما يُعلم بالضرورة ودرك الحواس لا يجوز أن يجتمع على جَحْدِه وإنكاره قوم بهم تثبت الحجة وينقطع العذرُ كما لا يجوز مثل ذلك في جحد وجودنا السماء فوقنا والأرض تحتنا وسماع كلام السائل لنا في هذا الباب; وفي جحد أكثر العالم والأمم لفعل الطباع والعلم بهذه الطبائع أصلاً دليل على جهل مدعي هذه الدعوى .
And if it were permissible for a claimant to claim that he knows the validity of the action of the natural property in the body regarding what occurs from these things by necessity, then it would be permissible for us to claim that we know the falsity of that claimant by necessity and that he is compelled to that. And there is no escape for them from this. And it has been established that what is known by necessity and sense perception cannot have a people by whom proof is established and excuse is cut off unite in denying it and rejecting it, just as such is not permissible regarding the denial of the existence of the sky above us and the earth beneath us and hearing the speech of the interlocutor to us in this chapter. And the denial of most of the world and nations of the action of natural properties and knowledge of these properties at all is evidence of the ignorance of the claimant of this claim.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage employs a reductio ad absurdum and an analogy. If the naturalist could claim necessary knowledge of natural causation, then we could equally claim necessary knowledge that he is false, which would create a contradiction. Moreover, truly necessary knowledge (like the sky being overhead) is such that no rational community can deny it. But we find that many people deny natural causation, proving it is not necessary. The widespread denial itself is evidence of the claimant's ignorance.

Key Terms
اضطرار
جحد
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge means that every single individual must accept it.
Correction: Baqillani shows that truly necessary knowledge cannot be denied by any rational community that serves as a proof, but it does not require universal acceptance by every individual; however, widespread denial by rational communities indicates it is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use as an example of something that is truly known by necessity?
A) The heat of fire.
B) The existence of the sky above us.
C) The action of natural properties.
D) The generation of effects.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes necessary knowledge of natural causation by showing it leads to contradiction and is denied by many rational people.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever been absolutely certain of something, only to find that intelligent people disagree, how does Baqillani's argument force you to re-evaluate the grounds of that certainty? What is one belief you hold that you must now test by this standard?
?
Consider a moment you attributed an outcome to a 'natural cause' without consciously acknowledging Allah's immediate control. How does this passage expose that as a failure of tawhid? What habit can you adopt to remind yourself that every effect is Allah's direct act?
?
Baqillani says that what is known by necessity cannot be denied by any sound-minded person. Is there any claim about religious truth that you think is so obvious that its denial is inexcusable? How would you defend that claim using Baqillani's method without falling into the same error he refutes here?

Arguments Against Necessary Knowledge of Causation
Baqillani cites consensus and analogy to refute necessary knowledge of causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that knowledge of causation is not necessary but rational, and his use of consensus and analogy to refute claims of necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowing the cause of an event is not a necessary perception but a rational inference.

In this passage, Baqillani challenges the idea that we can know the cause of events through direct necessity. He points to widespread disagreement and consensus among theologians that origination from non-existence is not known necessarily. Then he draws a clever analogy with Mu'tazili claims of 'generation' to show the absurdity of claiming necessary knowledge of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Notice the rhetorical questions and the shift to the analogy.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two main arguments: first, the denial of necessary knowledge of specific events and the consensus against necessary knowledge of origination; second, an analogy with Mu'tazili generation.
3
Understand the consensus claim
Baqillani asserts that all theologians agree that the occurrence of a thing from non-existence is not known by necessity. This undermines the claim of necessary knowledge of causation.
4
Analyze the analogy
Baqillani compares the claim of necessary knowledge of causation to a Mu'tazili claim of necessary knowledge of generated effects, implying both are equally baseless.
The text is cut off, but the implication is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أن كثيراً من المتكلمين ينكر العلم بوجود حوادث هي إحراق وإسكار من جهة الاضطرار فكيف يعلم حدوثها من مُحْدِث بعينه وعن شيء بعينه اضطراراً؟ وكثير من الناس يجهلون وجود هذه الأعراض وأعيانها فكيف يُضطرون مع ذلك إلى العلم بفاعلها؟ وعلى أن سائر المتكلمين وأهل التحصيل قد أطبقوا على أن حدوث الشيء وكونه عن عدم لا يُعلم اضطراراً ; فكيف يُعلم مِمَّن حدث؟ وعن أي شيء حدث اضطراراً؟ والعلم بحدث الشيء وما حدث عنه فرغ للعلم بأنه مُحْدَث; فإذا لم يَعْلَمُه مُحْدَثاً، اضطراراً، ولم يشاهده معدومـاً قبل وجوده وموجـوداً بعد عـدمـه، فكيف يُضطر إلى العلم بمُحْدِثه، لولا الغفلة والذهاب عن التحصيل؟. ويُقال لهم في هذا أيضاً لو قال لكم قـائـل من المعتزلة القائلين بالتولد: إنني أعلم حدوث الألم وذهاب السهم والحجر متولدين عن الرمي والدفع والاعتماد وكذلك الكسر والقطع وتأليف الأجسام عند حركات البنائين واعتمادهم ; وإنني أشاهده وأحسه اضطراراً، هل كان في دعواه ذلك إلا بمنزلتكم في إدعاء فعل الطباع ؟ لأنه يقول : وَجَدْتُ هذه الأمور
And moreover, many theologians deny knowledge of the existence of events that are burning and intoxication by way of necessity, so how can one know their occurrence from a specific originator and from a specific thing by necessity? And many people are ignorant of the existence of these accidents and their essences, so how are they compelled, despite that, to knowledge of their agent? And moreover, all theologians and people of accomplishment have agreed that the occurrence of a thing and its being from non-existence is not known by necessity. So how can one know from whom it occurred? And from which thing it occurred by necessity? And knowledge of the occurrence of the thing and from what it occurred is a branch of knowledge that it is originated. So if one does not know it as originated by necessity, and does not observe it as non-existent before its existence and existent after its non-existence, how can one be compelled to knowledge of its originator, were it not for heedlessness and departure from investigation? And it is said to them in this also: if a speaker from the Mu'tazila who believe in generation said to you: 'I know the occurrence of pain and the going of the arrow and stone as generated from shooting and pushing and reliance, and similarly breaking, cutting, and composition of bodies at the movements of builders and their reliance; and I witness it and sense it by necessity,' would his claim be in any station other than your station in claiming the action of natural properties? Because he says: 'I have found these things...'
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage combines multiple arguments. First, many theologians deny that burning and intoxication are known necessarily, so the claim of necessary knowledge of their cause is unfounded. Second, there is consensus that the origination of something from non-existence is not known necessarily; thus, knowledge of the cause is derivative and not necessary. Third, the author presents an analogy: if a Mu'tazili claimed necessary knowledge of generated effects (e.g., pain from shooting), would that be any different from the naturalist's claim? The text ends with the Mu'tazili's assertion, but the implication is that both claims are equally baseless.

Key Terms
محدث
حدوث
تولد
بنائين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Baqillani denies any knowledge of causation, but he only denies that it is necessary and self-evident.
Correction: Baqillani's argument is that knowledge of causation is rational and acquired, not given by sense perception or necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the consensus among theologians according to Baqillani?
A) Burning is caused by fire.
B) The occurrence of a thing from non-existence is not known by necessity.
C) Accidents can act.
D) Generation is true.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani argues that knowledge of causation is not necessary but rational, using consensus and analogy to refute claims of necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself saying, 'I know this happened because of that' with absolute certainty, without pausing to consider that your knowledge is not necessary but a rational inference? What does that reveal about your reliance on your own perception over divine causality? Commit today to one intentional moment of attributing an effect directly to God as the true originator.
?
The passage warns that claiming necessary knowledge of causation stems from heedlessness. In what areas of your daily life are you most prone to such heedlessness—assuming you know the cause of your successes, failures, or natural events? Write down one specific situation where you will actively remind yourself that God alone is the ultimate cause.
?
Baqillani compares the Mu'tazili claim of generation to the naturalist's claim of nature acting. How often do you unconsciously treat natural laws, habits, or human abilities as independent agents? What would change in your reliance on worldly means if you fully internalized that only God has direct causal power? Name one practical step you will take today to live this conviction.

Why Nature's Actions Cannot Be Known Necessarily
Baqillani argues that actions attributed to nature cannot be known necessarily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that knowledge of nature's actions (e.g., burning, intoxication) cannot be necessary (ḍarūrī) and how this refutes the claim that causality is known instinctively.

Core Idea
If something were known necessarily, everyone would agree—but they don't.

Baqillani tackles a key claim of natural causality: that burning and intoxication are known necessarily from natures. He shows this cannot be true because necessary knowledge is universal and undeniable, yet many people are ignorant of these causes. He also appeals to scholarly consensus that coming-into-existence itself is not known necessarily, so its cause cannot be either.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Baqillani's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim being refuted
Baqillani refutes those who claim that actions like burning and intoxication are known necessarily (ḍarūran) to be caused by natures (ṭabā'i').
This is a specific claim about necessary knowledge of causality.
3
Follow Baqillani's first argument: necessary knowledge is universal
He argues that what is known necessarily cannot be denied by a community that establishes proof. Since many deny or are ignorant of these causal relations, they cannot be necessary.
Key point: disagreement disproves necessity.
4
Notice the consensus argument
Baqillani states that most mutakallimūn agree that coming-into-existence (ḥudūth) itself is not known necessarily, so knowledge of the specific agent cannot be necessary either.
This strengthens his case by appealing to scholarly agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيلة لهم في الخلاص منه، وقد ثبت أن ما يُعلم بالضرورة ودرك الحواس لا يجوز أن يجتمع على جَحْدِه وإنكاره قوم بهم تثبت الحجة وينقطع العذرُ كما لا يجوز مثل ذلك في جحد وجودنا السماء فوقنا والأرض تحتنا وسماع كلام السائل لنا في هذا الباب؛ وفي جحد أكثر العالم والأمم لفعل الطباع والعلم بهذه الطبائع أصلاً دليل على جهل مدعي هذه الدعوى . وعلى أن كثيراً من المتكلمين ينكر العلم بوجود حوادث هي إحراق وإسكار من جهة الاضطرار فكيف يعلم حدوثها من مُحْدِث بعينه وعن شيء بعينه اضطراراً؟ وكثير من الناس يجهلون وجود هذه الأعراض وأعيانها فكيف يُضطرون مع ذلك إلى العلم بفاعلها؟ وعلى أن سائر المتكلمين وأهل التحصيل قد أطبقوا على أن حدوث الشيء وكونه عن عدم لا يُعلم اضطراراً ; فكيف يُعلم مِمَّن حدث؟ وعن أي شيء حدث اضطراراً؟ والعلم بحدث الشيء وما حدث عنه فرغ للعلم بأنه مُحْدَث; فإذا لم يَعْلَمُه مُحْدَثاً، اضطراراً، ولم يشاهده معدومـاً قبل وجوده وموجـوداً بعد عـدمـه، فكيف يُضطر إلى العلم بمُحْدِثه، لولا الغفلة والذهاب عن التحصيل؟
A trick for them to escape from it. It has been established that what is known necessarily and by perception of the senses is not permissible for a people by whom the proof is established and the excuse is cut off to gather upon denying and rejecting it, just as the like of that is not permissible in denying our existence: the sky above us and the earth beneath us and hearing the speech of the questioner to us in this chapter. And in the denial of most of the world and nations of the action of natures (tabā'i'), and knowledge of these natures at all, is evidence of the ignorance of the claimant of this claim. And also that many of the mutakallimūn deny knowledge of the existence of events that are burning and intoxication from the aspect of necessity; so how can its coming into existence be known from a specific producer (muḥdith) and from a specific thing necessarily? And many people are ignorant of the existence of these accidents and their essences; so how can they be compelled, despite that, to knowledge of their agent? And also that the rest of the mutakallimūn and people of verification have agreed that the coming into existence of a thing and its being from non-existence is not known necessarily; so how can it be known from whom it occurred? And from which thing did it occur necessarily? And knowledge of the occurrence of a thing and what it occurred from is a branch of knowledge that it is produced (muḥdath); so if he does not know it as produced, necessarily, and does not observe it as non-existent before its existence and existent after its non-existence, how can he be compelled to knowledge of its producer (muḥdith), were it not for heedlessness and departure from verification?
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that actions like burning and intoxication are caused necessarily by natures (tabā'i'). Baqillani argues that such knowledge cannot be claimed as necessary (ḍarūrī) because: 1) Necessary knowledge (e.g., sky above, earth below) cannot be denied by a community whose testimony establishes proof; yet nature advocates deny that many people are ignorant of these causes. 2) Many mutakallimūn deny that such events are known necessarily at all, so how can their specific cause be known necessarily? 3) The majority of mutakallimūn agree that coming into existence (ḥudūth) itself is not known necessarily; thus, knowledge of the agent is also not necessary. The passage concludes that claiming necessary knowledge of a specific producer is due to heedlessness.

Key Terms
الضرورة
الطباع
المتكلمون
مُحْدَث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that we know necessarily (ḍarūran) that fire burns by nature, without any inference.
Correction: Baqillani argues that this cannot be necessary knowledge because many people are ignorant of or deny such causal relations; necessary knowledge is universal and undeniable.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani reject the claim that actions like burning and intoxication are known necessarily from natures?
A) Because these actions are not real.
B) Because many people are ignorant of them, so they cannot be necessary knowledge.
C) Because all mutakallimūn deny necessary knowledge of any event.
D) Because natures do not exist.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that claims of necessary causal knowledge are invalid because such knowledge would be universally held, which it is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you felt absolutely certain that one event caused another—like a fire burning your hand. Did you arrive at that certainty through reasoning, or did you assume it was self-evident? How does Baqillani's argument challenge that assumption, and what does that mean for how you understand God's role in every event?
?
If most people—including many scholars—disagree on whether we know causes necessarily, can you really claim that your own causal judgments are beyond doubt? Are you content to rely on heedless habit rather than careful verification?
?
This lesson argues that ignorance of the true producer (God) often comes from heedlessness. Where in your daily life do you most naturally see the world as self-causing, forgetting the Creator? What would it take to turn every event into a reminder of Him?

Refutation of Tawallud through Parity with Nature Argument
Baqillani refutes tawallud by showing its logical parity with the nature argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud by demonstrating its logical equivalence to the nature (tabā'i') argument, and why both fail due to occasional absence of effects.

Core Idea
Claims of necessary causality collapse when effects occasionally don't follow.

Baqillani now takes on the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud (generation of acts). He argues that if the Mu'tazilites claim necessary knowledge that acts like throwing generate pain, that claim is indistinguishable from the nature advocates' claim that fire burns by its nature. By showing that both claims are undermined when the expected effect fails to occur, Baqillani levels a powerful critique against any theory of necessary causal connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the parallel
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how Baqillani sets up a hypothetical Mu'tazilite statement and compares it to the nature claim.
Focus on the structure of the argument, not the details.
2
Understand the parity argument
Recognize that Baqillani argues both sides rely on claimed necessary knowledge from observation, yet effects are not always observed.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Analyze the counter-argument and rebuttal
The nature proponents object that effects are not always present due to hindrances. Baqillani applies the same logic to tawallud, showing no distinction.
Note the symmetry: both sides use the hindrance excuse.
4
Summarize the refutation
Conclude that neither tawallud nor nature theories can prove necessary causality; the occasional absence of effects disproves the claim.
This is the takeaway for the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُقال لهم في هذا أيضاً لو قال لكم قـائـل من المعتزلة القائلين بالتولد: إنني أعلم حدوث الألم وذهاب السهم والحجر متولدين عن الرمي والدفع والاعتماد وكذلك الكسر والقطع وتأليف الأجسام عند حركات البنائين واعتمادهم ; وإنني أشاهده وأحسه اضطراراً، هل كان في دعواه ذلك إلا بمنزلتكم في إدعاء فعل الطباع ? لأنه يقول : وَجَدْتُ هذه الأمور تحدث عند الرمي والاعتماد والضرب والرجم والصحة وأحس ذلك فيتعلق في ذلك بمثل ما به تعلقتم ; فإن سوّغوه ذلك، صاروا إلى إثبات التولد وتركوا القول بفعل الطباع وإن امتنعوا منه لم يجدوا إلى الفصل سبيلا . وتُعارضُ المعتزلة في القول بتولّد هذه الأمور عن الصحة والضرب والرمية بقول المثبتين للطباع فلا يجدون في ذلك فصلاً . وإن قال أصحاب الطبائع : قد تُوجد هذه الحركات والاعتمادات أحياناً غير متولدة لما ادعته المعتزلة، فبطل أن تكون متولدة في حال من الأحوال، يقال لهم : وكذلك قد يوجد تناول الشراب ومجاورة النار أحياناً مع عدم الإحراق والإسكار، فبطل أن يكون الإحراق واجباً عن فعل الطباع . فإن قالوا : إنما تفعل طباعُ الأغذية والأدوية مع عدم المانع لها ، قيل : وكذلك إنما تتولد هذه الأسباب مع عدم الموانع من مسبباتها ; ولا فصل في ذلك .
And it is said to them in this also: If a speaker from the Mu'tazila who say tawallud said to you: 'I know the occurrence of pain and the going of the arrow and the stone as generated (mutawallid) from throwing, pushing, and leaning, and likewise breaking, cutting, and composing bodies at the movements of builders and their leaning; and I observe it and sense it necessarily,' would his claim in that be anything other than your status in claiming the action of natures (tabā'i')? Because he says: 'I found these things occurring at the time of throwing, leaning, hitting, stoning, and health, and I sensed that.' So he would cling in that to the like of what you clung to. If they permit that for him, they would come to affirming tawallud and abandon the statement of the action of natures; and if they refrain from it, they find no way to distinguish. And the Mu'tazila are contradicted in the statement of generation of these things from health, hitting, and throwing by the statement of the affirmers of natures, and they find no distinction in that. And if the proponents of natures say: 'Sometimes these movements and leanings occur without generating what the Mu'tazila claimed, so it is invalid that they are generated in any state,' it is said to them: Likewise, sometimes the consumption of drink and proximity to fire occur without burning and intoxication, so it is invalid that burning is necessary from the action of natures. And if they say: 'The natures of foods and medicines only act when there is no hindrance for them,' it is said: Likewise, these causes only generate when there are no hindrances to their effects; there is no distinction in that.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud (generation of acts) by drawing a parallel with the nature (tabā'i') claim. Baqillani first constructs a hypothetical: if a Mu'tazilite claimed necessary knowledge that pain, movement of arrows, etc. are generated from acts like throwing and leaning, this claim would be identical to the nature advocates' claim of necessary knowledge that burning and intoxication come from natures. If nature advocates accept this reasoning, they must accept tawallud; if they reject it, they have no basis to distinguish their own claim. Then Baqillani uses a standard kalam argument: the effect is not observed every time the cause is present (e.g., throwing does not always cause pain), so the link is not necessary. Similarly, consumption of drink does not always cause intoxication, so the nature claim fails. The counter-argument that natures act only in the absence of hindrances is met with the same response for tawallud, showing no distinction.

Key Terms
التولد
الطباع
المعتزلة
الموانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that tawallud and nature are entirely different theories, so refuting one does not affect the other.
Correction: Baqillani shows that both rely on the same claim of necessary causal connection from observation, and both are refuted by the occasional absence of the effect.

Checkpoint
What is the main parallel Baqillani draws between the Mu'tazilite claim of tawallud and the claim of those who assert nature (tabā'i')?
A) Both claim that the effect is created by God directly.
B) Both claim necessary knowledge of a causal link that is not always observed.
C) Both deny the existence of accidents.
D) Both rely on scriptural evidence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud is refuted by the same logic that refutes nature theories: the effect is not always present when the cause is present, so the causal link is not necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a setback despite your best efforts, do you automatically assume that your actions should have produced a certain result? How does this lesson challenge your assumption of necessary causality in your daily life?
?
Think of a time when you felt entitled to a particular outcome because of your actions. How does Baqillani's argument expose the flaw in that entitlement? What does it mean for your reliance on God?
?
Do you ever find yourself trusting in 'natural laws' more than in God's direct control? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that only God creates effects?

Refuting the Fifth Nature Claim
Baqillani dismisses the claim of a fifth nature as baseless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's criticism of the claim that the celestial sphere has a fifth nature, and why he considers it baseless without evidence.

Core Idea
Extraordinary claims about celestial nature require evidence, not mere observation of motion.

In this passage, Baqillani takes on a philosophical claim that the celestial sphere is made of a fifth nature beyond the four elements. He first presents the argument of those who assert this: the sphere's perpetual circular motion cannot be explained by the elemental natures, so it must have a unique nature. Then he directly challenges the basis of this reasoning, demanding proof for why circular motion implies a fifth nature. This lesson shows how Baqillani applies critical thinking to cosmological theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani uses a question-and-answer style to challenge the claim.
2
Identify the opponents' argument
Find where Baqillani presents the reasoning of those who claim a fifth nature: they observe perpetual circular motion and inability to move up/down or rest, and infer a unique nature.
This is the core of their case.
3
Analyse Baqillani's critique
Notice that Baqillani does not provide a counter-argument but instead questions the inference itself. He asks for evidence, implying the claim is unsupported.
This is a typical kalam method: challenging the opponent's premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قول كثير من هؤلاء إن للفلك طبيعة خامسة ليست بحرارة ولا برودة ولا رطوبة ولا يبوسة، فإنه أيضاً قول باطل لا حجّة عليه ; فيقال لهم : لِمَ قُلْتُم ذلك؟ وما دليلكم عليه ? فإن قالوا : لأنا وجدنا الفلك يتحرك حركة دورية أبداً سرمداً ولا يصح أن يتحرك في جهات العالم الست ولا أن يقف ويسكن بدلاً من الحركة فوجب أن تكون له طبيعة خامسة لأن الدال على طبائع الأجسام حركاتها في جهة العلو والسفل، فيقال لهم : لم قلتم هذا؟ وما دليلكم؟
As for the statement of many of these that the celestial sphere (falak) has a fifth nature that is not heat, cold, moisture, or dryness, it is also a false statement with no proof upon it. So it is said to them: Why did you say that? And what is your evidence for it? If they say: Because we found the celestial sphere moving with a perpetual, eternal circular motion, and it is not valid for it to move in the six directions of the world, nor to stop and be still instead of moving, so it must have a fifth nature because that which indicates the natures of bodies is their movements in the direction of up and down. It is said to them: Why did you say this? And what is your evidence?
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the celestial sphere has a fifth nature (distinct from the four elements). Baqillani states that this claim is false and lacks evidence. He then presents the argument of those who assert it: the sphere moves perpetually in a circle, cannot move in the six directions (up, down, etc.), and cannot rest, so its motion indicates a unique nature because the movements of bodies in up/down indicate their elementary natures. Baqillani responds by questioning the basis of this inference: why should movement in a circle require a fifth nature? The passage ends by demanding evidence, indicating that the claim is gratuitous.

Key Terms
طبيعة خامسة
الفلك
حركة دورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Baqillani denies the celestial sphere has any unique properties, or that he rejects the observation of circular motion.
Correction: Baqillani does not deny the observed motion; he denies the unjustified inference that this motion requires a fifth nature. He objects to the philosophical notion of an inherent fifth nature, not to the sphere's unique behavior itself.

Checkpoint
What is the basis Baqillani reports for the claim of a fifth nature for the celestial sphere?
A) The celestial sphere is made of fire.
B) Its perpetual circular motion and inability to move in up/down directions.
C) The celestial sphere has no motion at all.
D) It is composed of the four elements.

Key Takeaway
The claim of a fifth nature for the celestial sphere is dismissed by Baqillani as unsubstantiated, emphasizing that theological cosmology must be based on evidence and sound reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you encounter arguments that seem logical but lack proof, do you challenge them like Baqillani, or do you accept them because they are common? Reflect on a specific instance and commit to one change.
?
What assumptions do you hold about the natural world that might be based on insufficient evidence? Are you willing to scrutinize them as Baqillani scrutinized the fifth nature claim? What is the cost of not doing so?

Refutation of Tawallud and the Theory of Natures
Baqillani refutes via dialectical symmetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses symmetrical arguments to refute both the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud and the theory of natures.

Core Idea
Baqillani shows that proponents of natures and tawallud face the same dilemma.

In this passage, Baqillani engages in a dialectical argument to expose the logical inconsistency in both the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud (generated effects) and the theory of natures (inherent causal powers). He shows that if one side is vulnerable to a certain objection, the other side is equally vulnerable. This symmetrical refutation is a hallmark of his method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
Read the passage and note the Mu'tazilite claim that actions like shooting generate health, and the proponents of natures' claim that natures cause effects necessarily.
The Mu'tazila affirm tawallud; the proponents of natures affirm fixed causal powers.
2
Follow the dialectical exchange
Trace each claim and counterclaim: Mu'tazila's initial position, the reply from proponents of natures, Baqillani's counter-reply, and the final appeal to obstacles.
Pay attention to how each side is forced into a similar position.
3
Notice the symmetry
Observe that Baqillani applies the same logical move to both theories: if one is invalid due to occasional non-occurrence, so is the other; if one appeals to obstacles, the other can too.
Baqillani does not defend either theory but shows their mutual inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحدث عند الرمي والاعتماد والضرب والرجم والصحة وأحس ذلك فيتعلق في ذلك بمثل ما به تعلقتم؛ فإن سوّغوه ذلك، صاروا إلى إثبات التولد وتركوا القول بفعل الطباع وإن امتنعوا منه لم يجدوا إلى الفصل سبيلا. وتعارض المعتزلة في القول بتولد هذه الأمور عن الصحة والضرب والرمية بقول المثبتين للطباع فلا يجدون في ذلك فصلا. وإن قال أصحاب الطبائع: قد توجد هذه الحركات والاعتمادات أحيانا غير متولدة لما ادعته المعتزلة، فبطل أن تكون متولدة في حال من الأحوال، يقال لهم: وكذلك قد يوجد تناول الشراب ومجاورة النار أحيانا مع عدم الإحراق والإسكار، فبطل أن يكون الإحراق واجبا عن فعل الطباع. فإن قالوا: إنما تفعل طباع الأغذية والأدوية مع عدم المانع لها، قيل: وكذلك إنما تتولد هذه الأسباب مع عدم الموانع من مسبباتها؛ ولا فصل في ذلك.
He speaks about shooting, leaning, striking, stoning, and health, and he senses that, so he attaches in that to something like that to which you attached. So if they permit that, they come to affirming generation (tawallud) and abandon the statement of the action of natures (al-ṭibāʕ). And if they refuse it, they find no way to differentiate. And the Mu'tazila are countered in their statement about the generation of these things from health, striking, and shooting by the statement of those who affirm natures (al-muthbitīn li-l-ṭibāʕ), so they find no differentiation in that. And if the proponents of natures (aṣḥāb al-ṭabā'iʕ) say: These movements and leanings are sometimes found not generated (ghayr mutawallida) as the Mu'tazila claimed, so it is invalid that they be generated in any state, it is said to them: Likewise, the consumption of drink and proximity to fire are sometimes found without burning and intoxication, so it is invalid that burning be necessarily due to the action of natures. And if they say: The natures of foods and medicines only act in the absence of an obstacle, it is said: Likewise, these causes only generate (tatwalladu) in the absence of obstacles to their effects; there is no difference in that.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage engages in a dialectical refutation between the Mu'tazila and the proponents of natures (ṭabā'iʕ) regarding the concept of tawallud (generation of effects). Baqillani first notes that the Mu'tazila claim that certain actions like shooting, leaning, etc., produce health. He says that if they permit this generation (that actions generate effects), they are effectively affirming tawallud and abandoning the theory of natures. If they refuse to permit it, they have no way to separate the cases. Then he says that the Mu'tazila are countered by the proponents of natures in that they cannot differentiate. Then he makes a symmetrical argument: if the proponents of natures say that movements are sometimes found not generated (i.e., not necessarily produced), just as the Mu'tazila claimed, then the Mu'tazila would have to accept that burning from fire and intoxication from drink are also not necessary, thus undermining the necessary action of natures. If the proponents of natures then say that natures act in the absence of obstacles, Baqillani replies that likewise, the causes produce effects only in the absence of obstacles. So there is no real difference between the theories; both face the same problem of universality in the presence of obstacles.

Key Terms
تولد (tawallud)
طباع (ṭibāʕ)
طبائع (ṭabā'iʕ)
متولد (mutawallid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Baqillani is defending the theory of natures against tawallud.
Correction: Baqillani is using dialectical symmetry to refute both theories; he does not side with either.

Checkpoint
What argument does Baqillani use to counter the claim that natures act only in the absence of obstacles?
A) He accepts it.
B) He says causes also require absence of obstacles.
C) He denies obstacles exist.
D) He calls it irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes both tawallud and the theory of natures by showing that each theory's objections apply equally to the other, leaving no logical basis to choose between them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a recovery from illness after taking medicine, do you instinctively attribute the cure to the medicine's 'nature' or to Allah's direct will? How does this lesson expose the flaw in assuming a necessary causal connection, and what would change if you truly internalized that Allah is the only real agent?
?
Think of a recent situation where you relied on a 'secondary cause'—a skill, a relationship, or a system. Did you consciously recognize that Allah could have created obstacles to its effect? How would your gratitude and reliance shift if you remembered that nothing acts except by His permission at every instant?
?
The argument shows that both tawallud and natures proponents end up relying on the same qualification (absence of obstacles). In your own reasoning about why things happen, do you ever cover your assumptions with an 'unless...' clause? How might you instead ground your trust directly in Allah's decree?

Refuting the Fifth Nature of the Celestial Sphere
Baqillani refutes the claim of a fifth nature through dialectical questioning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's dialectical refutation of the claim that the celestial sphere has a fifth nature, and his argument that lack of observed change does not prove impossibility.

Core Idea
Constant circular motion does not necessitate a fifth nature.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani takes on the claim that the celestial sphere must have a fifth nature because of its perpetual circular motion. He systematically questions the basis of this claim, showing that the proponents' reasoning is flawed. By the end, he exposes their inability to prove that the sphere cannot change its motion or that it moves by itself without a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the back-and-forth dialectical structure.
This passage is a series of questions and answers.
2
Identify the proponents' argument
The proponents claim the sphere has a fifth nature because it moves circularly and never stops. Underline how they connect motion direction to nature.
They argue from observed constancy.
3
Trace Baqillani's counters
Baqillani first questions the need for a proof, then argues that lack of observed change does not prove impossibility. Find where he says 'your finding that this is impossible today does not prove its impossibility'.
This is a key logical point.
4
Reflect on the final challenge
Baqillani concludes by demanding proof for a self-moving mover, which the proponents cannot provide. Consider why this undermines their entire position.
This connects to the broader kalam argument for a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قول كثير من هؤلاء إن للفلك طبيعة خامسة ليست بحرارة ولا برودة ولا رطوبة ولا يبوسة، فإنه أيضاً قول باطل لا حجة عليه؛ فيقال لهم: لم قلتم ذلك؟ وما دليلكم عليه؟ فإن قالوا: لأنا وجدنا الفلك يتحرك حركة دورية أبداً سرمداً ولا يصح أن يتحرك في جهات العالم الست ولا أن يقف ويسكن بدلاً من الحركة فوجب أن تكون له طبيعة خامسة لأن الدال على طبائع الأجسام حركاتها في جهة العلو والسفل، فيقال لهم: لم قلتم هذا؟ وما الحجة فيه؟ وما يدريكم أن الفلك لا يجوز أن يسكن يوماً ما ولا أن يتحرك حركة مستقيمة في إحدى الجهات الست وإن كنتم لم تجدوا ذلك قط؟ فإن قالوا: لأن ذلك لو جاز لجاز أن يتحرك الماء والأرض سَوْمَ أنفسهما إلى فوق، وأن تتحرك النار سَوْمَ نفسها إلى أسفل، قيل لهم: ما أنكرتم من جواز ذلك، إن كان ها هنا متحرك يتحرك سَوْمَ نفسه بغير محرك يحركه؟ وما وجودكم لتعذر هذا اليوم ما يدل على استحالته. ثم يقال لهم: ما الدليل أولاً على أن ها هنا متحركاً يتحرك سَوْمَ نفسه بغير محرك يحركه ويخترع فيه الحركة أو من غير أن يكون قادراً على تحريك نفسه ومختاراً لذلك؟ فلا يجدون إلى تصحيح ذلك سبيلاً.
As for the statement of many of these that the celestial sphere (al-falak) has a fifth nature (ṭabīʿa khāmisa) that is neither heat, nor cold, nor moisture, nor dryness, it is also a false statement with no proof for it. So it is said to them: Why did you say that? And what is your proof for it? So if they say: Because we found the sphere moving perpetually in a circular motion, while it cannot move in the six directions of the world, nor can it stop and rest instead of motion, so it must have a fifth nature, because what indicates the natures of bodies is their movements in the direction of above and below; it is said to them: Why did you say this? And what is the proof for it? And how do you know that the sphere is not permitted to come to rest someday, nor to move rectilinearly in one of the six directions, even if you have never observed that? So if they say: Because if that were permitted, then it would be permitted for water and earth to move by themselves upwards, and for fire to move by itself downwards; it is said to them: What do you deny about the permissibility of that, if there is a mover moving by itself without a mover moving it? And your finding that this is impossible today does not prove its impossibility. Then it is said to them: What is the proof first that there is a mover moving by itself without a mover moving it and creating motion in it, or without it being capable of moving itself and choosing that? So they find no way to verify that.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that the celestial sphere has a 'fifth nature' distinct from the four elements. Baqillani begins by stating the claim is baseless. He then questions the proponents: Why do you say this? They respond that the sphere always moves circularly, never rectilinearly, and never stops, so it must have a different nature because natures are inferred from motion direction (up/down). Baqillani counters: How do you know it cannot stop or move rectilinearly? Even if you haven't observed it, that doesn't prove impossibility. They argue that if it could move rectilinearly, then water and earth would move up and fire down, violating observed natures. Baqillani asks: What prevents that possibility if there is a mover moving by itself without an external mover? He then challenges: How do you prove the existence of such a self-moving mover? They cannot prove it, so their argument collapses.

Key Terms
فلك (falak)
طبيعة خامسة (ṭabīʿa khāmisa)
حركة دورية (ḥaraka dawriyya)
سَوْمَ نفسه (sawma nafsihī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The celestial sphere must have a fifth nature because it always moves circularly.
Correction: Baqillani shows that constant circular motion does not prove a fifth nature; it could be caused by an external mover, and we cannot rule out that the sphere could eventually change its motion.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani question the claim that the sphere cannot move rectilinearly?
A) Because it contradicts the Qur'an.
B) Because lack of observation does not prove impossibility.
C) Because Aristotle said it can.
D) Because it would imply a fifth nature.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demolishes the claim of a fifth nature by showing that the proponents cannot prove that the sphere cannot change its motion, nor can they prove that it moves by itself without a mover.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume something is impossible simply because you have never seen it happen? What in your life might God change that you think is fixed?
?
If you truly believed that all motion depends on God's constant creation, how would that change your view of your own actions? Are you ever tempted to think you move independently?
?
Are you willing to let go of your natural assumptions about how the world works, even when they seem obvious? What would it cost you to admit that you don't know what the sphere might do tomorrow?

Reductio on Uniform Natures from Motion
Baqillani refutes the motion-based argument for natures via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses reductio ad absurdum to refute the claim that motion direction determines the nature of elements.

Core Idea
If motion direction defines nature, then all upward-moving bodies share one nature—a contradiction to standard elemental theory.

Baqillani here employs a classic kalam method: he takes the opponent's principle to its logical extreme to show it leads to absurdity. The opponent claims that the direction of motion reveals the nature of an element. Baqillani shows that this would force fire and air to have the same nature (both go up), and water and earth to have the same nature (both go down), which contradicts the standard view that they are distinct. Then he anticipates a counterargument and refutes that too.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Focus on the logical steps Baqillani takes.
Notice the 'then it is said to them' structure introducing the reductio.
2
Identify the two reductions
Find where Baqillani shows that uniform motion leads to uniform nature for fire/air and for water/earth.
Look for 'فيجب استواء طبعهما' and 'يجب اتفاق طبع'.
3
Notice the contradiction
Contrast the conclusion of uniform nature with the standard elemental properties (moist/dry) that the opponents hold.
Baqillani explicitly says 'وهذا ترك لقولهم'.
4
Analyze the objection and reply
Read the objection about earth being closer to the center, and Baqillani's counter that this would imply different natures for earth layers—an absurdity.
This shows the reductio is thorough.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: فيجب على اعتلالكم هذا استواء طبع الهواء والنار والماء والأرض لأن النار والهواء يتحركان أبداً صُعُداً سَوْمَ أنفسهما ولا يتحركان في غير هذه الجهة فيجب استواء طبعهما لاستواء حركتيهما إن كان اختلاف الحركات دالا على اختلاف الطباع؛ وكذلك يجب اتفاق طبع الماء والأرض لاتفاق حركتيهما إلى جهة السفل وهذا ترك لقولهم إن الماء رطب والأرض يابسة والهواء رطب والنار يابسة فإن قالوا: الأرض أقرب حركة إلى المركز من الماء فوجب اختلاف طبعيهما، قيل لهم: فوجب مخالفة طبع الصفحة التي نحن عليها من الأرض لطبع الصفحة التي تلي المركز لأن ذلك أقرب حركة إلى المركز؛ وكذلك القول في تركيب الصفحات وهذا ترك قولهم.
Then it is said to them: According to your reasoning, the nature of air, fire, water, and earth must be uniform, because fire and air always move upwards by themselves and do not move in other than this direction, so their natures must be uniform due to the uniformity of their motions, if differences in movements indicate differences in natures. Likewise, the nature of water and earth must be the same due to their shared movement towards the direction of below. This refutes their statement that water is moist and earth is dry, and air is moist and fire is dry. So if they say: Earth is closer in motion to the center than water, so their natures must differ; it is said to them: Then the nature of the layer of earth that we are on must differ from the nature of the layer that adjoins the center, because that one is closer in motion to the center. The same applies to the arrangement of layers. This refutes their statement.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage uses a reductio ad absurdum against the proponents of natures. Baqillani says: If the nature of a body is determined by its direction of motion (up or down), then since air and fire both move upwards, they must share the same nature. Similarly, water and earth both move downwards, so they must share the same nature. This contradicts their claim that air is moist, fire is dry, water is moist, earth is dry. If they try to differentiate by saying earth is closer to the center in motion than water, so their natures differ, Baqillani argues that then different layers of earth would have different natures (since lower layers are closer to the center), which is absurd. Thus their theory is inconsistent.

Key Terms
طبع (ṭabʕ)
استواء (istiwāʾ)
اختلاف الحركات (ikhtilāf al-ḥarakāt)
مخالفة (mukhālafa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani is denying that bodies have any natures at all.
Correction: Baqillani is not denying natures; he is showing that the specific criterion of motion direction leads to absurd consequences if used to differentiate natures.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani argue follows from the claim that motion direction determines nature?
A) Air and fire have the same nature.
B) Water and earth have different natures.
C) All four elements have the same nature.
D) The sphere has a fifth nature.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the motion-based criterion for different natures is self-defeating because it forces contradictory outcomes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that a seemingly obvious principle (motion direction determines nature) leads to absurdity when followed consistently. Is there a belief you hold about Allah or His creation that you have never tested to its logical conclusions? Will you dare to follow it out, even if it exposes a contradiction in your own thinking?
?
Baqillani refuted a false criterion for distinguishing natures. In your own life, what false criteria do you use to judge the value of actions or people—like outward appearances, wealth, or emotional reactions? Will you accept the logical consequence of those criteria, or will you abandon them for the truth taught by revelation?

Questioning Self-Motion and the Reductio on Elemental Natures
Baqillani opens with a dialectical challenge and then reduces the opponents' position to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's dialectical method in refuting the claim that bodies move by their own nature, and see how he reduces the opponents' position to absurdity regarding the elements.

Core Idea
If motion reveals nature, then fire and air share one nature—contradicting the opponent's own claims.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani begins to dismantle the doctrine of natural motion by first questioning the very possibility of something moving by itself. He then forces his opponents to confront the logical consequences of their own reasoning: if differences in motion indicate differences in nature, then fire and air, which both move upward, must have identical natures. This directly contradicts their own assertions about the qualities of the elements.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening dialectical challenge
Read the Arabic and translation of the first part, where Baqillani questions how the opponents know the celestial sphere cannot rest or move rectilinearly. Notice his appeal to the limits of experience.
He is not denying observation, but arguing that lack of experience does not prove impossibility.
2
Identify the opponents' claim and Baqillani's counter
The opponents assert that if such motion were possible, then water/earth could move up and fire down by themselves. Baqillani turns this around: if self-motion exists, why deny it?
He then demands proof for the very premise of self-motion.
3
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
Follow Baqillani's argument that if motion differences indicate nature differences, then fire and air (both upward) must have the same nature, and water/earth (both downward) must have the same nature—negating the opponents' distinctions.
4
Analyze the reply about closeness to center
The opponents try to argue that degrees of closeness to the center differentiate water from earth. Baqillani shows this leads to an infinite regress of natures among layers of earth, which also negates their position.
This ends the first part of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة فيه؟ وما يدريكم أن الفلك لا يجوز أن يسكن يوماً ما ولا أن يتحرك حركة مستقيمة في إحدى الجهات الست وإن كنتم لم تجدوا ذلك قط؟ فإن قالوا : لأن ذلك لو جاز لجاز أن يتحرك الماء والأرض سَوْمَ أنفسهما إلى فوق، وأن تتحرك النار سَوْمَ نفسها إلى أسفل، قيل لهم : ما أنكرتم من جواز ذلك، إن كان ها هنا متحرك يتحرك سَوْمَ نفسه بغير محرك يحركه؟ وما وجودكم لتَعَذَّر هذا اليوم ما يدل على استحالته. ثم يقال لهم : ما الدليل أولاً على أن ها هنا متحركاً يتحرك سَوْمَ نفسه بغير محرك يحركه ويخترع فيه الحركة أو من غير أن يكون قادراً على تحريك نفسه ومختاراً لذلك؟ فلا يجدون إلى تصحيح ذلك سبيلاً.

ثم يقال لهم : فيجب على اعتلالكم هذا استواء طبع الهواء والنار والماء والأرض لأن النار والهواء يتحركان أبداً صُعُداً سَوْمَ أنفسهما ولا يتحركان في غير هذه الجهة فيجب استواء طبعهما لاستواء حركتيهما إن كان اختلاف الحركات دالا على اختلاف الطباع؛ وكذلك يجب اتفاق طبع الماء والأرض لاتفاق حركتيهما إلى جهة السفل وهذا ترك لقولهم إن الماء رطب والأرض يابسة والهواء رطب والنار يابسة. فإن قالوا : الأرض أقرب حركة إلى المركز من الماء فوجب اختلاف طبعيهما، قيل لهم : فوجب مخالفة طبع الصفحة التي نحن عليها من الأرض لطبع الصفحة التي تلي المركز لأن ذلك أقرب حركة إلى المركز؛ وكذلك القول في ترتيب الصفحات وهذا ترك قولهم.

ويقال لهم : ويجب على اعتلالكم هذا أن تقضوا على اتفاق طبع كلية الماء والأرض والنار والهواء لأن من طبع كليات هذه الأشياء الوقوف في عالمها الذي هو موضع مركزها وأن لا تتحرك عنه فإن صاروا إلى ذلك تركوا قولهم، وإن قالوا : اتفاق كليات هذه الأشياء في السكون في مراكزها ومواضع كلياتها لا يدل على اتفاق طبعها قيل لهم : وكذلك اختلاف حركات جزئياتها الموجودة في عالمنا لا يدل على اختلاف طباعها ولا فصل في ذلك.

ويقال لهم أيضاً: يجب على موضوعكم هذا أن يكون طبع جزئيات هذه الأشياء مخالفاً لكلياتها وأن تكون طباعها خلاف طباع كلياتها؛ وذلك أن من شأن هذه الجزئيات الموجودة في عالمنا الحركة ومن شأن كلياتها السكون والوقوف فيجب لذلك اختلاف طباع الجزئيات والكليات؛ فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا قولهم؛ وإن أبوه قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً من اتفاق طبع الفلك والنار والهواء والماء والأرض وإن اختلفت حركات هذه الأمور؟ فلا يجدون إلى دفع ذلك سبيلاً.
What is the proof for it? And how do you know that the celestial sphere cannot come to rest one day, nor move in a straight line in one of the six directions, even if you have never observed that? If they say: because if that were possible, then water and earth could move upwards by themselves, and fire could move downwards by itself. It is said to them: Why do you deny that possibility, if there exists here something that moves by itself without a mover that moves it? And your experience of its impossibility today does not prove its impossibility. Then it is said to them: First, what is the proof that there exists something that moves by itself without a mover that moves it and invents motion in it, or without being able to move itself and choosing that? Then they can find no way to verify that.

Then it is said to them: According to this reasoning of yours, it would be necessary that the nature of air, fire, water, and earth be the same, because fire and air always move upward by themselves and do not move except in that direction; therefore their natures must be the same since their motions are the same, if differences in motion indicate differences in natures. Similarly, it would be necessary that the natures of water and earth be the same because their motions are the same downward. This negates their statement that water is moist and earth is dry, and air is moist and fire is dry. If they say: Earth is closer in motion to the center than water, so their natures must differ. It is said to them: Then the nature of the surface we are on must differ from the nature of the surface adjacent to the center, because that one is closer in motion to the center. And the same applies to the arrangement of the surfaces. This negates their statement.

And it is said to them: According to this reasoning of yours, you must judge that the universal natures of water, earth, fire, and air are the same, because it is the nature of the universals of these things to rest in their own world, which is the place of their center, and not to move from it. If they adhere to that, they negate their own statement. If they say: The agreement of the universals of these things in resting at their centers and their universal places does not indicate agreement in their natures. It is said to them: Likewise, the difference in the movements of their particulars existing in our world does not indicate a difference in their natures, and there is no distinction in that.

And it is also said to them: According to this principle of yours, the nature of the particulars of these things must be different from their universals, and their natures must be contrary to the natures of their universals. That is because the property of these particulars existing in our world is motion, and the property of their universals is rest and stillness, so the natures of the particulars and universals must differ. If they go along with that, they negate their own statement. If they refuse, it is said to them: Then why do you also deny that the natures of the celestial sphere, fire, air, water, and earth are the same, even if the motions of these things differ? Then they can find no way to repulse that.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the doctrine that bodies have inherent natures (ṭabā’iʿ) that cause their characteristic motions. Baqillani engages with opponents who claim that the elements (fire, air, water, earth) move by their own essence without an external mover. He begins by questioning the basis for denying that the celestial sphere could ever rest or move rectilinearly, pointing out that lack of experience does not prove impossibility. He then challenges the very premise that there is something moving by itself without a mover. 

Next, he reduces their position to absurdity: if differences in motion indicate differences in nature, then since fire and air both move upward, their natures must be identical; similarly water and earth both move downward, so their natures must be identical. This contradicts the opponents' own claims about the qualities moist/dry. If they try to avoid this by citing degrees of closeness to the center, Baqillani shows that this leads to an infinite regress of differing natures among layers of earth. 

He then extends the argument to universals (kulliyyāt) versus particulars (juz’iyyāt). If universals of the elements rest at their centers while particulars move, then by the opponents' logic the natures of universals and particulars would have to differ, contradicting the idea of a single nature per element. Finally, he forces them to either accept that all elements have the same nature (since they all have a universal nature of resting) or admit that differing motions do not prove differing natures. The passage ends by asserting that the opponents have no way to refute this.

Key Terms
طبع (ṭabʿ) - nature
سَوْمَ نفسه (sawma nafsihi) - by itself
حركة (ḥaraka) - motion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Baqillani denies observed regularities of motion, but he is only rejecting the metaphysical claim that these regularities arise from inherent natures without divine action.
Correction: Baqillani's argument is epistemological: lack of experience does not prove impossibility, and the opponents cannot prove self-motion exists.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what follows if one claims that differences in motion indicate differences in nature?
A) Fire and air must have the same nature because they both move upward.
B) Water and earth must have different natures because they both move downward.
C) The celestial sphere must have a nature different from all elements.
D) The universals of elements must move differently from their particulars.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses the opponents' own premise—that motion differences reveal nature differences—to force them into absurd conclusions about the elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the regularity of nature—like fire always burning or the sun always rising—do you attribute it to inherent properties or to the constant will of Allah? How does your answer shape your reliance on Him in daily life? Commit this week to consciously remind yourself before each routine event: 'This is not by nature, but by Allah's ongoing power.'
?
Baqillani challenges the assumption that past observation proves necessity. In your life, where have you assumed that certain outcomes are inevitable based on past patterns, forgetting that Allah has power over all things? Identify one area where you have fallen into this assumption and write a specific dua asking Allah to change that pattern if He wills.
?
If you truly internalized that nothing moves or acts by its own nature but only by Allah's constant creation, how would your attitude toward prayer and supplication change? Would you pray with more urgency for things you consider 'natural'? This week, choose one routine event (e.g., cooking, healing) and before it happens, pause and explicitly ask Allah to bring it about, acknowledging your total dependence.

Universals, Particulars, and the Collapse of the Natures Doctrine
Baqillani uses the universal-particular distinction to force a final reductio on the opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the distinction between universal and particular natures to further undermine the theory of inherent natures, and how his final reductio leaves the opponents without a coherent position.

Core Idea
If universals rest and particulars move, then natures must differ across levels—destroying the theory.

Having reduced the opponents' argument to absurdity regarding the elements, Baqillani now deploys a more sophisticated argument based on the distinction between universal natures (kulliyyāt) and particular instances (juz’iyyāt). He shows that if the universals of the elements rest at their centers while the particulars move in our world, then by the opponents' own logic, the natures of universals and particulars would have to differ—contradicting the idea of a single nature per element. This forces the opponents into a dilemma: either accept that all elements share the same nature (since their universals all rest), or admit that differing motions do not prove differing natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about universal natures
Read the Arabic and translation of the section starting with 'ويقال لهم : ويجب على اعتلالكم هذا أن تقضوا على اتفاق طبع كلية...' Pay attention to the concept of kulliyya (universal) and juz’iyya (particular).
Baqillani is now considering the natures of entire species, not just individual instances.
2
Understand the dilemma presented
If universals of elements rest at their centers, then they share the same nature (rest). But their particulars move, so their natures would differ. The opponents must either accept that all elements have the same universal nature (contradicting their claim) or reject that motion differences indicate nature differences.
3
Trace the final reductio
Baqillani extends this: if the opponents refuse to accept that differing motions don't indicate differing natures, then they must accept that the celestial sphere, fire, air, water, and earth all have the same nature, since they can all rest in their proper places. This is clearly absurd to them.
The passage ends with the assertion that they have no way to refute this.
