Refuting Celestial Causation: The Contact Argument
Baqillani refutes planetary causation via the contact requirement for generated action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that celestial bodies cannot cause events in the world because they lack the necessary contact required for generated action (tawallud).

Core Idea
No contact means no causation; planets cannot affect us because they never touch us.

Baqillani begins by dismantling the astrological claim that celestial bodies have causal power over earthly events. He appeals to a principle accepted by some theologians of his time—that generated action (tawallud) requires physical contact. Since planets neither touch us nor anything that touches us, they cannot produce effects through generation. This leads to a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the astrological claim
Read the first sentence to understand what astrologers say about lunar and solar positions causing events.
Note they claim the sun's position in a minute of the moon's constellation is influential.
2
Understand the principle of tawallud
Baqillani states that an agent acting through generation must touch the object or something that touches it. This is key.
3
Apply the principle to planets
Notice how he argues that since planets do not touch us, they cannot generate effects. Then he uses a reductio: if they could, we could affect them without contact, which is impossible.
4
Conclude the refutation
The final sentence declares that planets have no action or effect whatsoever. Reflect on the strength of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برج القمر وكائنةً في الدقائق التي يكون فيها القمر، أن يكون كون الشمس في تلك الدقيقة من فلكها ومن البروج مؤثراً لذلك الحادث متى وُجد، سواء كان في مقابلة الكائن فيه شيء أو لم يكن، وسواء رَبّعَ الكوكب أو سدسه لأن الكوكبين في ذلك المكان لا شك من جنس واحد فبطل بذلك ما قالوه . وعلى أن الفاعل في غيره على سبيل التولد لا يفعل فيه إلا بأن يماسه أو يماس ما ماسه ؛ ومحال عند أصحاب التولد أن يُخترع فيه الفعل اختراعاً بغير مماسة له ولا مماسة لما ماسه فيجب إذا كان كذلك ألا يصح فعل هذه الأفلاك فينا وتأثيراتها في أنفسنا وعالمنا إلا بأن تماسنا أو تماس ما ماسنا لأنه لو جاز أن تفعل فينا بالتولد على غير هذه السبيل لجاز وصح أن نفعل نحن أيضاً فيها تأثيرات وحوادث من غير أن نماسها أو نماس ما ماسها وفي تعذر ذلك واستحالته دليل على استحالة فعل هذه الكواكب فينا على هذه السبيل وإذا بطل ذلك أنه لا فعل ولا تأثير لهذه الكواكب والأفلاك بحال بتةً . فأما من أقر منهم بالإسلام وأذعن لحدوثها وأنها متعلقة بمحدث أحدثها وزعم أن الله تعالى جعلها دِلالة على ما يحدث في العالم في أوقاته فإنه أيضاً خبط وتخليط لأن الدليل المتعلق بمدلوله لا بد أن تكون جهة تعلقه به معروفة معلومة كجهة تعلق الكاتب بالكتابة وبكون صانعها عالماً، ودلالة الحوادث على حدوث ما لا يسبقها ولا يفنك منها ودلالة المعجزات على صدق صاحبها وأمثال ذلك مما قد عُرف جهة تعلق الدليل فيه بمدلوله ولا وجه من قبله يُعْلَم لِدَلالة كون هذه الأفلاك في البروج وسيرها وحركاتها على حدوث ما يحدث من الأمطار والنماء والنقصان وغلاء الأسعار وسفك الدماء وسكون الهيج والفساد وعلى ما يَسْتَسِر الناسُ بعلمه وما ينطوون عليه . وقد أخبر الله تعالى عن كذب مُدَّعى علم ذلك، وأنه تعالى المستبد بعلم ما كان ويكون فقال : «وأنبئكم بما تَأْكُلُونَ وَمَا تَدْخِرُونَ فِي بيوتكم». فجعل ذلك من دليل النبوة وما لا يطلع عليه إلا من أوجي به إليه وقال : «إِنَّ اللَّهَ عِنْدَهُ عِلْمُ السَّاعَةِ ويُنَزِّلُ الغَيْثَ وَيَعْلَمُ مَا فِي الأَرْحَامِ وَمَا تَدْرِي نَفْسٌ ماذا تَكْسِبُ غَداً وَمَا تَدْرِي نَفْسٌ بِأَيِّ أَرْضِ تَمُوتُ» ; وقال : «عالم الغيب فلا يظهر على غيبه أحداً إلا ارتضى من رسول» . وفي نظائر هذه الآيات ما يدل على أن علم ما يكون لا يدركه إلا علام الغيوب أو من أطلعه على ذلك ; فكيف يُدْرَك ذلك بقطع الأفلاك وسير النجوم؟ وكيف يجتمع في قلب مؤمن تصديق الرسل وتصحيح الآيات اعتقاد تصحيح أحكام المنجمين واعتقاد كون سير الأفلاك أدلة على علم ما كان ويكون ؟ وقد روي عن النبي أنه قال
The moon's constellation and its being in the minutes in which the moon is, that the sun's being in that minute of its orbit and of the constellations is an influence for that event whenever it occurs, whether or not there is anything in opposition to the thing in it, and whether the planet is in quartile or sextile because the two planets in that place are undoubtedly of one kind. Thus what they said is invalidated. And [the argument] that the agent in another by way of generation (tawallud) does not act in it except by touching it or touching what touches it; and it is impossible according to the proponents of generation that action be invented in it by invention without touching it or touching what touches it. So if this is the case, then it must be that the action of these celestial spheres in us and their effects in our souls and our world are not valid except by their touching us or touching what touches us, because if it were permissible for them to act in us by way of generation in this other way, then it would also be permissible and correct for us to act in them with effects and events without touching them or touching what touches them. And the impossibility and impossibility of that is evidence for the impossibility of the action of these planets in us in this way. And when it is invalidated that there is no action or effect whatsoever for these planets and celestial spheres. As for those among them who professed Islam and acknowledged their temporal origination and that they are attached to a temporal originator who originated them, and claimed that God Almighty made them signs for what occurs in the world at its times, this also is confusion and mixing, because the sign connected to its signified must have a known and understood aspect of its connection to it, like the aspect of the connection of the writer to writing and of its maker being knowledgeable, and the indication of events to the occurrence of what does not precede them and is not free of them, and the indication of miracles to the truthfulness of their possessor, and similar examples where the aspect of the connection of the sign to its signified is already known. And there is no aspect from which it is known for the indication of the being of these celestial spheres in the constellations and their course and movements to the occurrence of what occurs of rains, growth, decrease, high prices, bloodshed, cessation of turmoil and corruption, and what people keep secret in their knowledge and what they conceal. And Allah Almighty has informed of the falsehood of those who claim knowledge of that, and that He Almighty alone has knowledge of what was and will be. He said: 'And I will inform you of what you eat and what you store in your houses.' So He made that a proof of prophethood and what no one is informed of except to whom it is revealed. And He said: 'Indeed, Allah [alone] has knowledge of the Hour and sends down the rain and knows what is in the wombs. And no soul perceives what it will earn tomorrow, and no soul perceives in what land it will die.' And He said: 'Knower of the unseen, and He does not disclose His unseen to anyone, except whom He has approved of among messengers.' And in similar verses there is indication that knowledge of what will be is attained only by the Knower of the unseen or one to whom He has disclosed it. So how can that be attained by cutting the celestial spheres and the course of the stars? And how can the belief in the correctness of the judgments of astrologers and the belief that the course of the celestial spheres are signs for knowledge of what was and will be come together in the heart of a believer who affirms the messengers and confirms the verses? And it has been narrated from the Prophet that he said...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of astrological claims. It is structured as a two-part argument followed by Quranic evidence. First, Baqillani argues against the causal efficacy of celestial bodies by appealing to the theory of generation (tawallud) held by some opponents. The key premise is that an agent acting in another through generation must be in direct or indirect contact with the object. Since celestial spheres are not in contact with us, they cannot generate effects in us. If they could, then we could also affect them without contact, which is impossible. Hence, they have no action or effect whatsoever. Second, he addresses those who concede that the heavens are created and set by God as signs for events. He argues that for a sign to be valid, the connection between sign and signified must be known. Known signs include a writer's connection to writing, or miracles to truthfulness. But there is no known connection between planetary positions and earthly events like rains, prices, or bloodshed. Third, he invokes Quranic verses that reserve knowledge of the unseen (including future events) exclusively for Allah. Verses from Surah Al-Imran, Luqman, and Al-Jinn are cited to show that even prophets only know what Allah reveals. Thus, astrologers' claims are false and incompatible with faith. Finally, he mentions a hadith condemning astrology, though the text is cut off.

Key Terms
تولد (tawallud)
مماسة (contact)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Astrologers claim celestial bodies cause events through some form of influence that does not require physical contact.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if we accept the principle of tawallud (generated action), then contact is necessary. Without contact, causation is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the basic requirement for an agent to cause an effect through generation (tawallud)?
A) The agent must be temporally prior to the effect.
B) The agent must be in direct or indirect physical contact with the object.
C) The agent must be a celestial body.
D) The agent must be intelligent.

Key Takeaway
Celestial bodies cannot cause earthly events because they lack the contact required for generated action (tawallud).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself attributing the events of your life—good or bad—to astrological signs, planetary positions, or 'luck'? How does Baqillani's argument force you to reconsider where you place your trust?
?
When you experience hardship, do you automatically seek out natural causes as ultimate explanations, forgetting that Allah alone is the Creator of causes and effects? How can this lesson reshape your reliance on Him?
?
If certain dates or celestial events make you feel uneasy or hopeful, what does that reveal about your heart’s attachment to created things? Will you commit to purifying your tawheed from such subtle associations?

Refuting Astrological Signification: The Known Connection Argument and Quranic Proofs
Baqillani refutes astrological signification and cites Quranic verses on knowledge of the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects the idea that celestial bodies are signs for earthly events, using the need for a known connection and Quranic evidence that knowledge of the unseen belongs only to Allah.

Core Idea
Astrological signs fail because the link to events is unknown; the Quran reserves knowledge of the unseen for Allah.

Having refuted causal influence, Baqillani now turns to those who concede that the heavens are created by God but claim they are signs (dilāla) for events. He argues that a valid sign must have a known aspect of connection, like a writer to writing or a miracle to truth. No such connection exists for planetary positions. Moreover, Quranic verses clearly state that knowledge of future events is exclusive to Allah, invalidating astrological predictions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the alternative claim
Some astrologers admit the heavens are created but say God made them signs. Baqillani tackles this.
He calls it 'confusion and mixing'.
2
Identify the criterion for valid signs
A sign must have a known aspect of connection to its signified. Examples: writer to writing, miracles to truth.
These connections are understood.
3
Apply the criterion to astrology
Baqillani asks: what known connection exists between planetary positions and rains, prices, secrets? None.
List the events: rains, growth, decrease, prices, bloodshed, etc.
4
Introduce Quranic evidence
He cites verses that reserve knowledge of the unseen to Allah. Surah Al-Imran, Luqman, and Al-Jinn are quoted.
Verse: 'Indeed, Allah [alone] has knowledge of the Hour...' and 'Knower of the unseen...'
5
Conclude the incompatibility
Affirming astrology contradicts faith in the messengers and the Quran. How can a believer accept both?
The hadith is referenced but cut off.

?

?
Baqillani says belief in astrologers cannot coexist with belief in the messengers. Is there any area where you trust human predictions—economic forecasts, medical prognoses, even your own self-assessment—more than you trust Allah's decree? How can you realign your heart to submit completely to the Knower of the unseen?

Baqillani's Proof That Light and Darkness Are Temporal
Baqillani proves that light and darkness are temporal and cannot be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani proves that light and darkness are temporal (not eternal) based on their opposition and change, and why they cannot be described with manifestation or concealment.

Core Idea
Light and darkness are not eternal because they change and are opposites.

This passage presents two arguments from Imam Baqillani to demonstrate that light and darkness are accidents that come into being and pass away. First, he uses their mutual opposition and the impossibility of coexistence, drawing an analogy with motion and rest. Second, he argues that manifestation and concealment are properties of bodies, not accidents. Let's examine these proofs step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage has two distinct arguments, and the second is incomplete.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main points are clear.
2
Identify the first argument from opposition
Find how Baqillani uses the fact that light and darkness are opposites and that a body can change from one to the other. This shows they cannot be eternal.
He uses the analogy of motion and rest.
3
Understand the analogy with motion and rest
Just as motion and rest cannot coexist and one replaces the other, so light and darkness replace each other. This temporal succession proves they are accidents (hawadith).
4
Consider the second argument about manifestation and concealment
Baqillani argues that manifestation (zuhur) and concealment (kumun) are attributes of bodies, not accidents. Therefore, light and darkness, being accidents, cannot be said to manifest or conceal themselves.
This argument is cut off but the key point is clear.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Despite the incomplete last sentence, the lesson is that light and darkness are temporal because they change, oppose each other, and are accidents subject to generation and corruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما يَدُلُّ على أنهما ليسا بقديمين فهو ما قدمناه من الدلالة على تضادهما وجواز كونِ الشَّخص مَرَّةً مُضِيئاً نيراً ومَرَّةً أسودَ مُظْلِماً وأنه لا يجوز أن يكون ضياءُ الجسم ونوره موجوداً به في حال وجود سواده وظلامه كما لا يجوز أن تَكُونَ حركته موجودةً في حال سُكونه فَوَجَبَ أَنهما يَحْدُثان ويتجدّدان على الأجسام ويبطل النُّورُ في حال وجودِ الظَّلام كما تَبْطُلُ الحركة عند مجيء السكون . وقد قام الدليل أيضاً على أنه لا يجوز عليهما ولا على غيرهما من الأعراض الظهور والكمون لأن الظهور خروجٌ إلى مكان والكمون انتقال عنـه وكون في غيره من الأماكن واستتار ببعض الأجسام والحركة والسكون والاستتار والظهور من صفاتِ الأجسام دون الأعراض لما قدمناه من قبل في باب إثبات الأعراض. وأما ما يَدُلُّ على أن النور والظلام وغيرهما من الأعراض لا يجوز أن
As for what indicates that they are not eternal, it is what we have previously presented of the evidence for their being opposite and the permissibility of a thing being at one time luminous, bright and at another time black, dark, and that it is not permissible that the body's brightness and light exist in it in the state of its blackness and darkness, just as it is not permissible that its motion exist in the state of its rest. So it is necessary that they occur and renew upon bodies, and the light ceases in the state of the existence of darkness, just as motion ceases at the arrival of rest. And evidence has also been established that it is not permissible for them nor for other accidents [to have] manifestation and concealment, because manifestation is an exit to a place, and concealment is a transfer from it and being in other places and concealment by some bodies, and motion and rest, concealment and manifestation are attributes of bodies, not accidents, as we have previously stated in the chapter on establishing accidents. And as for what indicates that light and darkness and other accidents are not permissible to
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage provides a second argument that light and darkness cannot be eternal. Baqillani points to the fact that light and darkness are opposites; a body can be light at one time and dark at another. This change shows they are not eternal because eternal things do not change from one state to another. If they were eternal, they would always be the same. Furthermore, light and darkness cannot coexist in the same body at the same time, just as motion and rest cannot. Therefore, they come into being (حدث) and cease. He then adds that manifestation (ظهور) and concealment (كمون) are attributes of bodies, not accidents, so light and darkness, being accidents, cannot be said to manifest or conceal. The passage ends abruptly, beginning a third argument that accidents (including light and darkness) cannot be [agents?], but the text is incomplete.

Accidents Cannot Be Agents: The Necessity of Life, Power, and Will
Baqillani argues that accidents cannot be agents by nature or choice, as agency requires life, power, and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents cannot be agents according to Baqillani, and the three essential attributes an agent must possess.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot act because they lack life, power, and will.

Baqillani presents a concise argument against the possibility of accidents (like light and darkness) being agents. He establishes that true agency requires three essential attributes: life, power, and will. Accidents, by their nature, cannot possess these, and thus cannot be causes of good or evil. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms and the logical flow.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Key Claim and Premises
Baqillani states that accidents cannot be agents. Identify the premises that support this claim: the definition of an agent, and the nature of accidents.
3
Understand Why Accidents Cannot Be Agents
Agency requires life, power, and will. Accidents lack these, and also cannot receive other accidents. Therefore, they cannot act by nature or choice.
This is the core argument.
4
Reflect on Implications for Causality
Consider how this argument challenges common views on causality. Accidents like heat or light are not true causes; only God creates effects.
This relates to the occasionalist worldview of Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على أن النور والظلام وغيرهما من الأعراض لا يجوز أن يكونا فاعلين بالطباع ولا بالاختيار لخير ولا شر ولا نفع ولا ضر فهو أن الدلالة قد قامت على أن الفاعل لا يكون إلا حياً قادراً مختاراً، وأن هذه الصفات مستحقة لمعاني توجد بالموصوف وسندل على ذلك فيما بعد إن شاء الله. وقد اتفقنا على استحالة قبول الأعراض للأعراض فبطل أن تكون فاعلة.
As for what indicates that light and darkness and other accidents are not possible to be agents by nature nor by choice, for good or evil, benefit or harm, it is that evidence has established that the agent is not except living, powerful, and willing, and that these attributes are entailed by meanings (maʿānī) that exist in the described. We will demonstrate that later, God willing. And we have agreed on the impossibility of accidents receiving accidents, so it becomes invalid that they are agents.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof that accidents (including light and darkness) cannot be agents (fāʿilūn). Baqillani states that agency requires three attributes: life (ḥayāh), power (qudra), and will (irāda). Accidents do not possess these attributes. Moreover, accidents cannot receive or bear other accidents (istiḥālat qabūl al-aʿrāḍ li-l-aʿrāḍ), which is a known principle in kalām. Therefore, they cannot be the cause of any action or effect. This refutes dualist claims that light and darkness, as accidents, can produce effects like good or evil.

Correction: Baqillani argues that accidents cannot be true agents; they are merely instruments through which God creates effects directly.

Refutation of the Motion Argument
Baqillani refutes the dualist motion argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's two-pronged refutation of the dualist proof based on motion, including the possibility of naturally stationary bodies and the logical inconsistency regarding light and darkness.

Core Idea
Motion differences don't prove distinct natures if whole categories are also stationary.

Baqillani challenges the dualists' assumption that differing motions imply differing natures. He proposes that some bodies may be naturally stationary, like air, and points out a logical flaw: if motion indicates difference, then the immobility of entire categories of light and darkness would indicate similarity. This section unpacks these two arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Baqillani presents two distinct arguments against the dualist motion proof.
The passage uses rhetorical questions to expose weaknesses in the dualist logic.
2
Analyze the first point: stationary bodies
Baqillani asks why there couldn't be bodies that are naturally stationary, neither rising nor falling, like air. This would undermine the binary classification based on motion.
Consider how this challenges the dualist assumption that all bodies are either light or heavy.
3
Analyze the second point: light and darkness
If different motions of particulars indicate different natures, then the immobility of the whole categories of light and darkness (in their own world) would indicate that they are similar and homogeneous.
This is a logical trap: the dualists' own reasoning is turned against them.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Baqillani concludes that the dualists must either abandon their claim or refute their own inference. This shows the weakness of the motion argument.
This lesson establishes that motion alone is not a reliable indicator of nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون في أجسام العالم ما طبعه الوقوف كالهواء وما جرى مجراه فيكون لا منحدراً ولا متصاعداً؟ وما أنكرتم إن دل اختلاف حركة جزئيات النور والظلام في هذا العالم لطلب المركز والشوق إلى كليهما على اختلاف جنسيهما، أن يدل وقوف كلية الظلام والنور في عالمها وموضع مركزهما على تماثلهما وتجانسهما واتفاق طباعهما؟ فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا قولهم؛ وإن أبوه نقضوا استدلالهم على اختلاف جنس النور والظلام وطباعهما باختلاف حركات جزئياتهما ثم يقال لهم في جواب الدلالة الثالثة التي هي عمادهم ومفزعهم: ما أنكرتم على اعتلالكم من أن يكون العالم بأسره من طبائع أربع حرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة على ما قاله الأطباء وأصحاب الطبائع؟ فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم وإن راموا فصلاً لم يجدوه. وإن هم قالوا: إن سائر الأجسام المركبة من الطبائع الأربع لا يخلو أن تكون ذوات ظل أو ليست بذوات
Then it is said to them: What prevents you from there being among the bodies of the world that which its nature is to be stationary, like air and what runs in its course, so that it is neither descending nor ascending? And what prevents you from the fact that if the difference in motion of the particulars of light and darkness in this world, seeking the center and yearning for both of them, indicates the difference of their two kinds, then the immobility of the whole of darkness and light in their world and the place of their center indicates their similarity, homogeneity, and agreement of their natures? For if they pass over that, they abandon their statement; and if they refuse, they refute their own inference of the difference of the kind of light and darkness and their natures by the difference of motions of their particulars. Then it is said to them in response to the third proof, which is their mainstay and refuge: What prevents you from your reasoning that the entire world is from four natures: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness, according to what the physicians and naturalists said? For if they pass over that, they abandon their religion, and if they seek a separation, they will not find it. And if they say: All bodies composed of the four natures are not free from possessing shadow or not possessing shadow...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage contains two refutations. First, the author refutes the second dualist proof by suggesting the possibility of bodies that are naturally stationary, neither rising nor falling, which would undermine the binary classification. He also points out a logical inconsistency: if differences in motion indicate different natures, then the fact that light and darkness as wholes are stationary in their own world would indicate they are similar. Then the author introduces a third refutation: the dualists' reasoning could just as easily support a theory of four elements (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) as proposed by physicians, which would contradict their dualism. The argument shows that the dualists' method of classification is arbitrary and can be used to support other worldviews.

Key Terms
الوقوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists believe that differences in motion always indicate differences in nature.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the same logic can be used to argue the opposite: if motion indicates difference, then lack of motion indicates similarity.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say about the nature of air in his first counterargument?
A) Air is light and always ascends.
B) Air is heavy and always descends.
C) Air is naturally stationary, neither ascending nor descending.
D) Air has no nature at all.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani turns the dualists' motion argument against them by proposing stationary bodies and a logical inconsistency, showing that motion alone does not prove distinct natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily judgments of people, do you focus on their outward actions (motions) and instantly classify them, ignoring the bigger picture of their overall state? Think of one person you've misjudged this way. How can Baqillani's warning about over-relying on partial evidence change your approach?
?
You often label yourself based on specific failures or successes—'I am not consistent in prayer' or 'I am a good person because I donate.' But what does the 'whole' of your life say? Are you willing to let the overall trend of your heart, rather than isolated incidents, define your relationship with Allah? What would that shift require from you today?
?
The dualists faced a contradiction they could not ignore. Is there a belief you hold about your own 'nature'—like 'I can't change' or 'I'm not spiritual'—that crumbles when you consider the full reality of how Allah created you and what He expects? Are you ready to let that contradiction force you to abandon that limiting belief?

Alternative Four-Element Classification
Baqillani proposes four natures as an alternative worldview

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the theory of four natures (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) to argue that the dualists' reasoning is arbitrary and could support conflicting worldviews.

Core Idea
The dualists' method of classification is arbitrary and can be used to support a four-element theory.

Baqillani presents a powerful counter-argument: if the dualists' reasoning is accepted, it could just as easily prove a four-element system (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) as proposed by physicians. This would undermine the dualist position entirely. The passage shows the reductio ad absurdum of the dualist methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative classification passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Baqillani shifts to the third proof and proposes a four-nature classification.
Notice that Baqillani is using the dualists' own method to generate a competing classification.
2
Identify the reductio ad absurdum
Baqillani argues that if the dualists accept their own reasoning, they would have to accept the four-element theory, which would destroy their dualism.
This is a classic logical move: showing that an argument leads to an absurd conclusion.
3
Consider the consequences
If the dualists reject the four-element theory, they would have to explain why their method does not apply consistently, which they cannot do.
Baqillani's challenge forces the dualists to either abandon their religion or fail to find a distinction.
4
Reflect on the arbitrariness
This argument exposes that the dualists' classification is not based on objective criteria but on arbitrary selection.
The lesson shows the power of the reductio method in theological debate.

Refuting the Dualists' Shadow Proof via the Four Natures
Baqillani refutes dualists' third proof by turning their shadow argument against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani turns the dualist's shadow argument against them by demonstrating that it implies light and darkness are composed of the four natures, thus destroying their claim of simplicity.

Core Idea
The dualist's shadow argument backfires: if shadow proves composition, then light and darkness themselves must be composite.

This passage presents a clever refutation of the dualists' third proof, which argued that because all bodies either have a shadow or not, they must originate from light and darkness. Baqillani first offers a parallel from the naturalists' theory of four natures, then turns the dualist's own logic against them, forcing them into a contradiction they cannot escape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the tight argumentative structure.
The passage is a single continuous refutation.
2
Understand the dualist's original proof
The dualists argued that all bodies either have a shadow or not, so they must be composed of light and darkness. This is the proof Baqillani is refuting.
Shadow here implies composition from light and darkness.
3
Follow Baqillani's first move: the four natures parallel
Baqillani points out that if the dualist accepts that the world is made of four natures (heat, cold, moisture, dryness), they abandon their own religion. If they reject it, they have no escape.
This is a rhetorical trap: either way, the dualist loses.
4
Trace the dualist's counter and Baqillani's response
The dualist might say: bodies composed of four natures still must have shadow or not, so they must be from light and darkness. Baqillani turns this back: all bodies that have shadow or not also are not free from the four natures, so light and darkness themselves must be composed of the four natures.
This forces a composition on light and darkness, which the dualist denies.
5
Grasp the significance
The dualist's own reasoning leads to a contradiction: if shadow indicates composition, then light and darkness (which cause shadow) must be composed as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم في جواب الدلالة الثالثة التي هي عمادهم ومفزعهم: ما أنكرتم على اعتلالكم من أن يكون العالم بأسره من طبائع أربع حرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة على ما قاله الأطباء وأصحاب الطبائع؟ فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم وإن راموا فصلا لم يجدوه. وإن هم قالوا: إن سائر الأجسام المركبة من الطبائع الأربع لا يخلو أن تكون ذوات ظل أو ليست بذوات ظل فوجب أنها من نور وظلام؛ قيل لهم: وجميع الأجسام التي لا تنفك من أن تكون ذوات ظل أو ليست بذوات ظل لا تخلو ولا تنفك من الطبائع الأربع فوجب أن يكون النور والظلام مركبا من الطبائع الأربع ولا فصل في ذلك.
Then it is said to them in answer to the third proof, which is their mainstay and refuge: What denies you concerning your reasoning that the whole world consists of four natures—heat, cold, moisture, and dryness—as the physicians and naturalists say? For if they pass over that, they abandon their religion; and if they seek a separation, they do not find it. And if they say: 'All bodies composed of the four natures cannot but be either with shadow or without shadow, so they must be from light and darkness,' it is said to them: 'And all bodies that are not free from being with shadow or without shadow do not lack nor are free from the four natures, so it is necessary that light and darkness are composed of the four natures—and there is no separation in this.'
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist's third proof (based on shadow). The author first offers a parallel from the naturalists: if the dualist accepts that the world is made of four natures, they abandon their own religion (dualist belief). If they reject it, they cannot escape. Then the dualist might counter: 'All bodies composed of the four natures either have a shadow or not, so they must be from light and darkness.' The author turns this back: 'And all bodies that are not free from having or not having a shadow also are not free from the four natures, so light and darkness themselves must be composed of the four natures.' This forces a contradiction on the dualist: their own reasoning leads to the conclusion that light and darkness are not simple but composite, which they deny.

Key Terms
طبائع أربع
ظل
نور
ظلام
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shadow argument is decisive for dualism and proves the existence of two principles.
Correction: Baqillani shows the argument cuts both ways: if shadow indicates composition from light and darkness, then light and darkness themselves must be composite from the four natures, which dualists deny.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude by turning the shadow argument against the dualist?
A) That light and darkness are simple
B) That the four natures are unreal
C) That light and darkness are composed of the four natures
D) That all bodies are made of air

Key Takeaway
The dualist's own reasoning forces the conclusion that light and darkness are composed of the four natures, which contradicts their claim of simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When defending a core belief, do you put your trust in arguments that could backfire? How can you ensure your foundation is built on what cannot be reversed?
?
If the dualists were unwilling to abandon their proof even when it led to absurdity, where in your own life might you be protecting a comfortable position instead of following the truth wherever it leads?

An Accident Cannot Generate a Substance
Refutes claim that the devil was produced from God's punishment (an accident).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an accident (ʿaraḍ) cannot be the efficient cause of a substance (jawhar) and how this principle refutes the claim that the devil originated from God's punishment.

Core Idea
An accident never generates a substance—they belong to different ontological orders.

This lesson takes up a specific theological objection: could the devil have originated from God's punishment? Imam Baqillani shows that punishment, as an accident, cannot produce a substance. By grounding the argument in the basic distinction between accidents and substances, we see why such a claim is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation closely. Let the author's words establish the framework.
Pay attention to the phrase 'على سبيل الابتداء للفعل والتوليد' – it means 'by way of initiating action and generation'.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the words ʿaraḍ (accident), jawhar (substance), and tawlīd (generation) in the Arabic. Understand that an accident is a quality that exists in a substance, while a substance is an independent entity.
3
Understand the logical principle
The author asserts a universal rule: it is impossible for an accident to originate a substance. This is not just about the devil; it applies to all substances. Reflect on why this is a foundational Ashʿarī principle.
Accidents are dependent and temporary; substances are independent and enduring. An accident cannot cause a substance because that would require the accident to exist prior to the substance, but accidents inhere in substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَأَمَّا حُدُوثُ الْفِعْلِ عَنْ عُقُوبَةٍ فَإِنَّهُ أَيْضاً بَاطِلٌ، لِأَنَّ الْعُقُوبَةَ الَّتِي ذَكَرْتُمْ لَوْ كَانَتْ ثَابِتَةً لَكَانَتْ فِعْلاً وَعَرَضاً مِنَ الْأَعْرَاضِ، وَمُحَالٌ وُقُوعُ شَخْصِ الشَّيْطَانِ أَوْ غَيْرِهِ مِنَ الْعَرَضِ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الِابْتِدَاءِ لِلْفِعْلِ وَالتَّوْلِيدِ، كَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ حُدُوثُ سَائِرِ الْأَشْخَاصِ مِنَ الْأَعْرَاضِ عَلَى هَذِهِ السَّبِيلِ.
As for the occurrence of the act from a punishment, it is also false, because the punishment that you mentioned, if it were established, would be an act and an accident (ʿaraḍ) among accidents, and it is impossible for the person (shakhṣ) of the devil or anything else to occur from an accident by way of initiating action and generation, just as it is impossible for all other persons (ashkhāṣ) to occur from accidents in this way.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that the devil (or any being) originates from God's punishment. The author states that the punishment itself is an act (fiʿl) and an accident (ʿaraḍ). An accident is a quality that inheres in a substance. The key point is that an accident cannot be the efficient cause (mubdiʾ) of a substance (jawhar) like the devil's person (shakhṣ). The author uses the principle: an accident cannot originate or generate (tawlīd) a substance. This is a standard Ashʿarī principle: only substances can generate substances, and accidents cannot produce substances. Therefore, the claim that the devil came from a punishment (an accident) is impossible.

Key Terms
العَرَضُ (accident)
الجَوْهَرُ (substance)
التَّوْلِيدُ (generation)
الشَّخْصُ (person/individual)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think an accident like 'punishment' is a real entity that can produce effects, such as creating a substance.
Correction: The author clarifies that accidents cannot generate substances; they are ontologically dependent and cannot serve as efficient causes of substances.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for the devil to originate from a punishment (an accident)?
A) Because the devil is a substance, and an accident cannot generate a substance.
B) Because punishment is not a real thing.
C) Because God directly creates everything.
D) Because the devil is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot generate substances; this principle refutes any claim that a substance originates from an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed a temporary emotional state—an accident—for a lasting flaw in your character? How does knowing that accidents cannot generate substances change how you view the roots of your own vices?
?
If only substances can produce substances, and only God is the ultimate Creator of substances, then where do you place your hope for real transformation—in fleeting circumstances or in the One who alone can create a new heart?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you sometimes attribute its origin to a mere accident like a punishment or a trial? How does this lesson demand that you redirect your gaze from secondary causes to the only true Source of all that exists?

Why Punishment Cannot Generate a Substance
Bāqillānī argues that punishment cannot be the origin of Shaytan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Bāqillānī argues that punishment, as an accident, cannot generate a substance like Shaytan.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot generate substances; punishment is an accident.

Bāqillānī tackles a specific objection: could Shaytan have originated from a punishment? He rules this out by appealing to a fundamental metaphysical principle. Accidents are dependent on substances and cannot produce them. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the claim that punishment is an accident.
Pay attention to the terms 'accident' (عرض) and 'person' (شخص).
2
Identify the Principle
Identify the key principle: accidents cannot generate substances. This is stated explicitly and reinforced by analogy.
3
Apply to the Case
Apply the principle to the specific case of Shaytan. Understand why the claim of origination from punishment is impossible.
This is a general rule in Islamic theology: substances are not produced by accidents.

Accidents are temporary and depend on substances; they cannot produce substances. This is a general metaphysical principle: substances cannot arise from accidents. Baqillani on Experimentation and Divine Wisdom
Baqillani refutes the claim that knowledge of poisons is gained by trial on human bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that knowledge of poisons comes from experimentation on human bodies is inconsistent with divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom precludes the need for harmful experimentation.

This lesson presents Imam Baqillani's refutation of those who claim that knowledge of harmful substances is acquired through trial and error on human bodies. He argues that this view undermines divine wisdom, as God could have directly imparted this knowledge without causing harm. The passage functions as an objection-and-answer, cutting at the root of empirical claims about necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: denial, objection, answer.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The passage presents an objection from those who claim knowledge comes from trial. Find how Baqillani responds. What is the core of his refutation?
His answer hinges on the concept of divine wisdom (hikmah).
3
Analyze the implications for divine wisdom
Consider why relying on experimentation would imply that God is not wise according to the objector's own standard. How does this argument rely on God's power and mercy?
Baqillani points out that a wise person would not let his loved ones risk harm if he could prevent it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشاهداتهم وسائر حواسهم ما يدل على ذلك أو يحس به معرفة ما يحتاج إليه من هذا الباب، ولا هو مما يُعرف باضطرار. فإن قالوا: إنما أدرك الناس ذلك قديماً وعرفوه بالامتحان والتجربة على أجسامهم وأجسام أمثالهم من نسل آدم عليه السلام قيل لهم: فهذا مخرج للقديم عن الحكمة لأنه قد كان قادراً عندكم وعندنا أن يعرفهم السمومات ويوقفهم على الاغتذاء بما فيه صلاح أجسامهم والأدوية التي عند تناولها تزول أمراضهم وأسقامهم فيغنيهم ذلك عن إتلاف أنفسهم وأمثالهم وذهاب كثير منهم بالامتحان وطول التجربة وليس بحكيم عندكم من قدر أن يوقف أولاده وضعفته ومن يحب مصلحته على تجنب ما فيه هلكته وتناول ما فيه سلامته وبقاء مهجته فلم يفعل وأحالهم على التجربة والامتحان الذي فيه عطب البعض منهم والبوار وهذا ما لا حيلة لهم فيه.
Their observations and the rest of their senses do not indicate that, nor does one perceive thereby knowledge of what is needed from this chapter, nor is it known by necessity. For if they say: 'People only realized that in ancient times and knew it by examination and experimentation on their bodies and the bodies of their likes from the progeny of Adam, peace be upon him,' it is said to them: 'This removes the Eternal from wisdom, because He was able, according to you and us, to make known to them the poisons and to inform them of nourishment that contains the well-being of their bodies and the medicines which, when taken, remove their illnesses and sicknesses, so that would suffice them from destroying themselves and their likes and the loss of many of them through examination and long experimentation. And it is not wise, according to you, that one who is able to inform his children, his weak ones, and those whose well-being he loves, to avoid what destroys them and to take what contains their safety and preservation of their life, yet does not do so, but refers them to experimentation and examination in which there is the perishing of some of them and destruction—and this is something for which they have no recourse.'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The author opens with a denial that knowledge of what humans need in this chapter (i.e., knowledge of foods, medicines, poisons) is acquired through sense observation or by necessity. Then he anticipates an objection: people might say they learned these things through trial and error on their own bodies over time. The answer is that this would be inconsistent with divine wisdom. If God could have given this knowledge directly and prevented harm, but instead left humans to learn by experimentation which leads to loss of life, then that would not be wise. The author argues that according to the objector's own standards, a wise person would inform his dependents directly rather than let them risk harm. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
حكمة (wisdom)
سمومات (poisons)
التجربة (experimentation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of harmful substances can only be known through trial and error on human bodies.
Correction: Baqillani argues that God could have given this knowledge directly, so reliance on experimentation that causes harm is not wise.

Checkpoint
What argument does the author make against the claim that knowledge of poisons comes from experimentation on human bodies?
A) It is impossible to know by experimentation.
B) It contradicts divine wisdom because God could have given the knowledge directly.
C) Experimentation is permissible if done on animals.
D) Knowledge of poisons is innate.

Key Takeaway
The claim that knowledge of poisons is gained by experimentation on human bodies is refuted because it would imply that God, who could have given direct knowledge, instead allowed harm, which is inconsistent with divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refutation of Animal Experimentation
Baqillani refutes the claim that knowledge is gained by experimentation on animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the objection based on animal experimentation fails, and why causing harm to animals is equally problematic as harming humans in this context.

Core Idea
Causing harm to animals for knowledge is no better than harming humans.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses an attempt to salvage empirical knowledge by proposing experimentation on animals. He demonstrates that the same ethical and theological problems apply: destroying animals is considered ugly (qabih), and God could have given the knowledge directly without causing harm. Furthermore, animals, like humans, feel pleasure and pain, so the objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that knowledge was gained by experimenting on animals instead of humans. Identify the animals mentioned (wolves, dogs, birds, etc.).
2
Understand the response
Baqillani argues that the problem remains: destroying animals is ugly according to the objector's own ethical framework. If God permits this but could have given knowledge directly, that would be foolish.
Note the term 'qabih' (ugly) – a key concept in Islamic ethics.
3
Consider the implication about animals
The author adds that animals also feel pleasure and pain, so they are on par with humans. This strengthens the argument against using them for experimentation.
This shows the consistency of the critique.
4
Reflect on divine wisdom
If God could have given knowledge without any harm, then allowing harm would contradict wisdom. Ask yourself: how does this connect to the broader theme of causality?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إنما أدرك علم ذلك بالامتحان على أجسام غير الناس من الحيوان نحو الذئاب والكلاب وأجناس الطير وغيرهم من الحيوان قيل لهم فالمسألة بحالها لأن إتلاف جميع الحيوان عندكم قبيح؛ فإذا أباحكم الله إتلاف بعضه بالتجربة والمحنة وهو قادر على توقيفكم على ما يغني عن إتلاف الحيوان فقد سفه على أوضاعكم وخرج على زعمكم عن الحكمة ولا فرق بين الناس في ذلك وبين كل حيوان يَلَذُّ ويَالَم.
For if they say: 'That knowledge was only realized by experimentation on the bodies of other than humans, from animals such as wolves, dogs, kinds of birds, and other animals,' it is said to them: 'The question remains the same, because destroying animals in general is, according to you, ugly. So if God allows you to destroy some of them by experimentation and trial, while He is able to inform you of what would suffice you from destroying animals, then He has acted foolishly in your view and departed, according to your claim, from wisdom. And there is no difference between humans in that and between every animal that feels pleasure and pain.'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objector proposes that knowledge was gained by experimenting on animals instead of humans. The author responds that the problem remains: destroying animals is considered ugly (qabih) according to the objector's own ethical framework. If God permits destroying some animals for experimentation when He could have given the knowledge directly, then that would be foolish and contrary to wisdom. Furthermore, animals also feel pleasure and pain, so they are equal to humans in this regard. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
قبيح (ugly/disgraceful)
المحنة (trial)
إتلاف (destroying)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that experimenting on animals is acceptable because animals are different from humans and do not have moral standing.
Correction: The author argues that destroying animals is considered ugly (qabih) and that animals feel pleasure and pain, so they are no different from humans in this regard. Thus the objection fails.

Checkpoint

A) Animals do not feel pain.
B) It is permissible to destroy animals.
C) Destroying animals is considered ugly, and God could have given the knowledge without it.
D) Animals are not suitable for experimentation.

Key Takeaway
Causing harm to animals for the sake of knowledge is ethically and theologically problematic, just as harming humans would be, because God could have provided knowledge without any harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The disparity of animal natures makes experimentation unreliable
Baqillani argues that animal natures differ from humans, so experiments on them are inconclusive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the vast differences between animal and human natures render animal experimentation useless for acquiring knowledge about human health.

Core Idea
Animals' natures are so diverse that experiments on them cannot inform human medicine.

Baqillani continues his refutation of those who claim knowledge can be gained through experimentation on animals. He challenges the assumption that animals are sufficiently similar to humans for such experiments to be meaningful. By pointing out the radical differences in nature, diet, and response to medicines, he demonstrates the impossibility of extrapolating from animal to human.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice how Baqillani poses a rhetorical question to his opponents.
The passage is a single sustained argument.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the key claim: experimentation on animals does not provide knowledge for humans because of differing natures.
3
Examine the examples
List the specific examples Baqillani gives: mountain goat eating snakes, gazelle eating colocynth, etc. See how each shows a substance harmful to humans.
These examples illustrate the principle.
4
Understand the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that it is impossible to find an animal with the same nature and diet as a human, so animal experiments are inconclusive.
This undermines the opponent's reliance on experimentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: ما أنكرتم من أن لا يحصل أيضاً لبني آدم علم ما يحتاجون إليه في هذا الباب بالتجربة على أجسام الحيوان سوى الإنسان؟ وذلك أن الحيوان مختلف الطباع والأغذية والأدوية وأن منه ما يُعالَج مرضه بما لو عولج به الإنسان لتلف ومنه ما يغتذي بما لو اغتذى به الإنسان أو أكل يسيره لتلف كالوعل الذي يأكل الحيات والظبي الذي الحنظل والوحش الذي لا يعمل في جسده شيء من خشاش الأرض وكالنَّعَم الذي يقتات التبنَ والقَتْ والسمك التي ترعى الطين وغيره ولو أكل الإنسان بعض هذه الأشياء لأدى إلى تلفه فمن أين لابن آدم بالحيوان الذي طبعه في التسوية مثل طبعه وغذاؤه مثل غذائه ودواؤه مثل دوائه مع اختلاف طبائعه وتباين تركيبه وشهواته ونفوره؟ فلا يجدون إلى دفع ذلك سبيلاً.
And it is said to them: 'What prevents you from saying that the children of Adam also do not obtain the knowledge they need in this chapter by experimentation on the bodies of animals other than humans? That is because animals have different natures, foods, medicines, and among them are those whose illness is treated with something that, if a human were treated with it, would perish; and among them are those who feed on something that, if a human fed on it or ate a little of it, would perish, like the mountain goat that eats snakes, the gazelle that eats colocynth, the wild beast that nothing from the ground's insects harms its body, and like the cattle that eat straw and chaff, and fish that graze on mud and other things. If a human ate some of these things, it would lead to his destruction. So where does the son of Adam find an animal whose nature in composition is like his nature, whose food is like his food, and whose medicine is like his medicine, given the differences in their natures and the disparity in their constitution, desires, and aversion? They will not find a way to refute that.'
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a further argument continuing the refutation. The author challenges the objectors: why should we assume that humans can learn from experiments on animals? He points out that animals vary greatly in their constitutions, diets, and medicinal reactions. What is beneficial or harmful for one species may be the opposite for another. For example, the mountain goat eats snakes, the gazelle eats colocynth (a bitter plant), and various animals eat things that would be lethal to humans. Therefore, there is no guarantee that an animal's reaction to a substance will match a human's. The author concludes that the objectors cannot find a way to refute this point.

Key Terms
طباع (natures/temperaments)
التجربة (experimentation)
اختلاف الطباع (difference of natures)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that animals are sufficiently similar to humans for experimentation to yield reliable knowledge about human medicine.
Correction: Baqillani shows that animals have radically different natures, diets, and responses to medicines, so experiments on them cannot be extrapolated to humans.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that experimentation on animals is not reliable for human knowledge?
A) Animals are too different from humans in nature and diet.
B) Animals cannot be experimented on.
C) The author does not accept animal experimentation.
D) Only humans have natures.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani argues that the profound differences in animal natures make experimentation on them useless for understanding human health.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Baqillani argues that a child of Adam cannot find an animal whose nature matches his own. When you trust medical advice derived from animal studies, are you ignoring the radical difference between their nature and yours? How can you justify that trust after reading this passage?
?
The passage mentions that what cures an animal can kill a human. Have you ever assumed a natural remedy or drug tested on animals is safe for you? What will you change in how you evaluate such treatments after understanding their fundamentally distinct natures?
?
Baqillani's argument is not just about animals but about the limits of human knowledge through experience. Are you prepared to admit that there are areas of life, especially health and medicine, where your so-called knowledge is actually conjecture? What would it mean for you to rely instead on revelation and divine guidance for such matters?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
Why Animal Experiments Don't Prove Causation
Baqillani argues that since animals cannot speak, we cannot know the effects of substances on them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Baqillani, animal experimentation cannot provide reliable knowledge about the effects of substances on humans, because animals cannot communicate their internal sensations.

Core Idea
Without speech, the animal's internal experience remains unknowable.

Baqillani presents a powerful objection to those who claim that empirical observation alone can establish causal relationships. He argues that testing substances on animals is insufficient because animals cannot tell us what they feel. This passage challenges the very foundation of empirical certainty in matters of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the rhetorical structure starting with 'ثم يقال لهم' as an objection.
2
Identify the argument's core premise
Find the key statement: because the animal is not a speaking being (ناطق), we cannot know the effects. This is the linchpin.
3
Trace the logic from premise to conclusion
The argument: herb tried on animal → observable damage → but internal sensation unknown → same could happen to humans → no certainty.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Why does this matter for causality? It shows that empirical observation alone, without testimony from the subject, cannot yield certain knowledge of cause and effect.
This connects to the overall theme of the book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: أليس قد تُجَرِّبُ الحشيشة على جسم بعض الحيوان فتُوَلِّدُ في كبده أو وَرَماً في طحاله أو تقطعاً في أمعائه وغير ذلك من الأدواء التي يَعْظُم شأنها، ويُخافُ التلف بها فلا يُعْلَمُ ما وَلَدَتْ تلك الحشيشة والثمرة لأنه ليس بناطق يذكر ما يجده ويخبر بسببه؟ فما يُؤْمننا أن يتناول من ذلك شيئاً فيُولّد مرضاً مثل الذي ولده في جسم الحيوان؟ فلا يَقْدِرُون على دفع ذلك بحجة.
Then it is said to them: 'Is it not that an herb may be tried on the body of some animal and it produces in its liver a swelling or in its spleen a tumor or a rupture in its intestines and other such diseases whose seriousness is great, and one fears destruction from them, yet it is not known what that herb and fruit produced because it is not a speaking being that mentions what it finds and informs of its cause? So what assures us that a person might take some of that and it produces a disease like the one it produced in the animal's body? They cannot refute that by any argument.'
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents yet another objection. The author asks: if an herb is tested on an animal and causes internal damage (e.g., to the liver, spleen, or intestines), we cannot know exactly what the herb did because the animal cannot speak and describe its symptoms. This means we cannot be sure that the same herb would cause the same disease in humans. Therefore, animal experimentation does not provide reliable knowledge. The author concludes that the objectors have no argument to answer this.

Key Terms
حشيشة (herb)
ناطق (speaking being)
داء (disease)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observing physical symptoms in animals is sufficient to determine the effect of a substance.
Correction: Baqillani argues that without the animal's verbal description of its internal state, the specific cause and effect remain unknown, making such observation insufficient.

Checkpoint
What problem does the author raise regarding experimentation with herbs on animals?
A) Animals do not eat herbs.
B) Animals cannot speak to describe what they feel.
C) Herbs are always safe.
D) The effects are always visible.

Key Takeaway
Animal experimentation cannot yield certain causal knowledge because animals cannot communicate their internal experiences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a study that claims a substance is safe based on animal tests, do you ever pause to consider that animals cannot tell us how they truly feel inside? How might this affect your confidence in such results?
?
Baqillani argues that without speech, we cannot know the cause of an effect. Think of a situation in your own life where you assumed you knew the cause of someone's actions without hearing their side. How could that assumption have misled you?
?
This passage challenges a deeply held modern belief in scientific certainty. Are you willing to admit that your knowledge might be more limited than you thought? What would it cost you to live with that uncertainty?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
7 min
Denial of Miracles by Unreliable Witnesses Is Not Evidence
Baqillani refutes the objection that denial by Jews and Christians disproves miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the denial of miracles by groups such as Jews and Christians does not constitute proof against the occurrence of those miracles, according to Baqillani's reasoning.

Core Idea
The deniers of miracles are either liars or absent; their denial carries no weight.

Baqillani addresses a common objection: that since Jews and Christians deny the miracles of Moses and Jesus, those miracles are doubtful. He shows that this objection fails because the deniers either reject the miracles entirely out of obstinacy or were not present to witness them. Their testimony is therefore unreliable and cannot serve as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of Baqillani's argument.
Notice how he first refutes the claim that deniers only dispute the miraculousness, then provides a second argument about absence.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Baqillani gives two distinct reasons why the denial is invalid. Find them and note how each is structured.
The first argument addresses the complete rejection of miracles, the second focuses on the lack of reliable witnesses.
3
Analyze the logic of the second argument
The second argument references the historical context of the Prophet's battles to show that Jews and Christians were not present to witness the miracles.
Consider why the absence of potential witnesses undermines the objection.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this defense of miracles fits into the larger kalam tradition of using rational and historical arguments to defend religious claims.
This passage exemplifies the method of responding to objections by examining the reliability of the critics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك على بطلان ما ادعِي ونقل لهما فلما لم يكن هذا عندنا وعندهم كذلك لم يكن في إنكار الفِرَق لِمَا قام الدليل على صحته وثبوته دليل على بطلانه وكذب ناقليه فإن قالوا ليس ينكر أحد ممن ذكرتم ظهور هذه الأمور على يدي موسى وعيسى وإنما ينكرون كونها معجزاً ويدعون أنها حيل وتخييل ومخاريق فبطل ما قَلَبْتُم به الاعتراض علينا قيل لهم : ليس الأمر على ما ظننتم لأن أكثر من ذكرناه بل الكل منهم يجحد هذه الأمور أصلاً وإن تعاطى المتحذلق منهم أحياناً تسليمه جدلاً والطعن فيه بادعائه أنه من ضروب السحر والحيل وغير ذلك فلا معنى لإنكارهم لذلك . وشيء آخر يبطل ما سألتم عنه وهو أنا لا ننكر تكذب الواحد والاثنين وجحد ما شاهدوه وطيَّه وكتمانه من المسلمين وغيرهم من سائر الملل وإذا كان ذلك كذلك وكنا نعلم أنه لم يحضر مع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في هذه المشاهد والغزوات من اليهود والنصارى والمجوس عدد ينقطع بهم العُذر ولا يجوز عليهم الكذب وطي ما شوهد بل لا نعلم أيضاً أنه حضر في أكثرها أحد منهم لما كان من إبعاده إياهم وإخراجهم عن تلك الديار وتحيزهم إلى حيث يمكنهم قتال ونصب راية حرب معه من الحصون والنواحي كخيبر وغيرها لم يجب أن يكون إنكار من أنكر ذلك من اليهود والنصارى حجة في إبطاله لأنهم إما ألا يكون حضر معه في أكثرها أحد منهم أصلاً أو أن يكون حضر منهم الواحد والاثنان والخمسة أو العدد الذي عليهم الكذب وعلى مثلهم افتعاله والكتمان لما وإذا كان ذلك كذلك بطل هذا الاعتراض بطلاناً ظاهراً.
That is a refutation of what they claimed and transmitted to them. Since this is not the case with us and with them, the denial by groups of what the proof has established as true and proven is not evidence of its falsehood and the lying of its transmitters. If they say: 'None of those you mentioned denies the appearance of these things at the hands of Moses and Jesus; they only deny that they are miracles and claim that they are tricks, illusions, and deceptions.' Then what you turned the objection against us is invalid. It is said to them: 'The matter is not as you thought, because most of those we mentioned—rather, all of them—deny these things entirely, even if the pedantic among them sometimes pretends to accept them dialectically and attacks them by claiming they are types of sorcery, tricks, and other things. So their denial of that has no meaning. And another thing invalidates what you asked about: We do not deny the lying of one or two individuals, and their denial of what they witnessed, and their concealing and hiding it, among Muslims and others from all religions. And if that is the case, and we know that there was not present with the Prophet (peace be upon him) in these battles and campaigns from the Jews, Christians, and Magians a number that would cut off the excuse and whose lying and concealing of what was witnessed would be impossible. Rather, we also do not know that any of them were present in most of them, because of his distancing them and expelling them from those lands and their gathering in places where they could fight and raise the banner of war against him, like fortresses and regions such as Khaybar and others. It does not require that the denial of those who deny that from the Jews and Christians be a proof for its invalidation, because either none of them were present with him in most of them at all, or there were present from them one, two, five, or a number who are prone to lying and fabricating and concealing what they heard. And if that is the case, this objection is clearly invalidated.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The author, Imam Baqillani, addresses the claim that since some groups (Jews and Christians) deny the miracles of Moses and Jesus, those miracles are therefore doubtful. He shows that this denial does not disprove the miracles because those groups either deny them entirely (out of obstinacy) or were not present to witness them. He then adds a second argument: the same groups are known to lie and conceal what they saw even among Muslims, so their denial carries no weight. Finally, he points out that in the case of Prophet Muhammad, Jews and Christians were not present at most of his battles to witness his miracles, so their denial of those miracles is irrelevant.

Key Terms
إنكار
دليل
معجز
حيل وتخييل
تكذيب
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The denial of a miracle by a group of people is sufficient to disprove its occurrence.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the character and presence of the denier matter; if they are obstinate or absent, their denial is not evidence.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the claim that denial by Jews and Christians disproves the miracles of Moses and Jesus?
A) The deniers were not present to witness the miracles.
B) The deniers are all known liars.
C) The deniers were forced to deny.
D) The deniers only denied the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The denial of miracles by unreliable or absent witnesses does not constitute evidence against their occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth because you relied on the testimony of someone who was not an eyewitness or who had a bias? How does this lesson challenge you to evaluate the sources of your own beliefs about the unseen? Will you commit to seeking firsthand knowledge or trustworthy accounts?
?
This lesson shows that denying a miracle based on the word of enemies is invalid. In your own life, when you hear objections to Islam from those who are hostile or absent, how does this lesson call you to respond? Will you investigate the truth yourself or remain influenced by such denial?
?
Consider the example of the Prophet Muhammad's miracles. Baqillani argues that the deniers were absent. Reflect on your own certainty in the prophethood of Muhammad. Have you ever doubted because of objections you heard? How can this teaching strengthen your faith and resolve to trust in the authentic proofs of Islam?
